Section 0

Strategic Overview

The SSI Index v4.0 answers the question "How healthy is this substation?" across 39,234 Italian distribution nodes. The SSI Enhanced Neural Network (SSI-ENN) extends this into three new questions: "What is a 4.9 MW or 9.9 MW BESS worth at this substation — to the DNO and to the community?", "What is the real option value of this BESS investment over 30 years?", and "Can we sell this intelligence as a recurring data service across all EU countries plus the UK?"

The product evolution follows a deliberate sequence: from measurement (the SSI Index) to prediction and pricing (the Neural Network) to monetisation (the data platform). Each phase builds on and validates the previous one. The SSI Index is the foundation layer — without substation-level grid intelligence, neither the BESS valuation nor the commercial platform can exist.

Strategic positioning: The SSI-ENN is not a battery sizing tool. It is a grid-intelligence-driven investment valuation platform. The two reference BESS configurations — 4.9 MW / 4hr and 9.9 MW / 4hr — are strategically chosen to sit below the two principal regulatory thresholds in European distribution grids. The 4.9 MW capacity sits below the 5 MW boundary that triggers additional technical requirements in most EU jurisdictions (UK ENA G99 Type B/C boundary, Germany §9 EEG direct marketing, several Italian comuni simplified permitting thresholds). The 9.9 MW capacity sits below the 10 MW boundary that triggers transmission-level connection processes (Italy AU threshold, UK distribution connection limit, France RTE/Enedis boundary, Germany §8 EEG). Both configurations ensure every modelled asset is distribution-connected and therefore maps directly onto the SSI's substation-level architecture. The engine is designed to accommodate arbitrary storage specifications beyond these two reference cases, as well as demand-side assets and eventually other flexibility technologies (V2G, hydrogen electrolysers, community solar+storage).

0.1   Reference BESS Specifications

The SSI-ENN models two reference configurations. Every substation receives parallel valuations for both, enabling developers and investors to compare the investment case at each scale.

ParameterConfig A (4.9 MW)Config B (9.9 MW)Rationale
Rated power4.9 MW9.9 MWConfig A below 5 MW: avoids UK G99 Type C requirements, German §9 EEG direct marketing, simplified permitting in several Italian comuni. Config B below 10 MW: avoids IT Autorizzazione Unica, UK transmission connection, FR RTE/Enedis boundary, DE §8 EEG threshold.
Duration4 hours (both configs)Qualifies for capacity market products in UK (T-4), IT (Terna Capacity Market), FR (mécanisme de capacité). Sufficient for peak-shifting and most ancillary service products.
Nameplate capacity19.6 MWh39.6 MWh= Rated power × 4 hr. Actual usable capacity degrades per the repowering curve.
Cycle constraint1 cycle/day (both configs)365 equivalent full cycles/year. Rules out multi-cycle arbitrage-heavy strategies. Positions the asset as grid-services-first, peak-shifting-second. Aligns with DNO flexibility procurement models.
Chemistry (baseline)LFP (LiFePO₄)Dominant chemistry for grid-scale BESS in 2025–2030 window. Superior cycle life (>6,000 cycles at 80% DoD) vs NMC. Lower energy density acceptable for stationary applications. No cobalt supply chain risk. Repowering decision points allow chemistry upgrades.
Round-trip efficiency (BoL)89%88%Config A slightly higher RTE due to lower C-rate and proportionally lower PCS losses. Both degrade ~0.3%/yr.
Project life30 years (both configs)Covers ≥2 module replacements (repowering at ~Year 12–13 and ~24–25). Balance-of-plant (inverters, transformers, civils) lifespan matches 30-year horizon.
Augmentation strategyFull repowering at capacity triggerReplace battery modules when usable capacity falls below 70% of nameplate. Decision points are real options (Phase 2). Config A repowering costs ~50% of Config B (linear with capacity).
Typical footprint~1,500 m²~3,000 m²Config A's smaller footprint broadens the set of viable substation locations, particularly in urban and peri-urban areas where land is constrained.
Dual-config value: Many substations will show a positive business case for Config B (9.9 MW) but not Config A (4.9 MW), or vice versa. A rural substation with high DER saturation but limited ancillary service access may favour Config A (lower capex, sufficient to absorb local curtailment). A peri-urban substation near a wholesale market node may favour Config B (economies of scale in ancillary service stacking). The SSI-ENN produces both valuations in parallel, enabling the platform to recommend the optimal configuration per substation — and to identify substations where neither configuration is viable.

0.2   System Architecture

The SSI-ENN is a five-layer architecture. Each layer corresponds to one project phase:

Layer 1 — Data SSI v4.0 Index · Market data · Grid topology · Regulatory parameters · Climate (CMIP6)
→
Layer 2 — Valuation BESS Value-Add Engine (DNO + Community) · Real Options Pricing (TCO + Revenue + Optionality)
→
Layer 3 — Neural Network Transfer learning: IT (full) → EU (partial) · Scalability engine · Uncertainty propagation
Layer 4 — Coverage 28 countries (EU27 + UK) · ~500K substations · Country-specific calibration
→
Layer 5 — Platform Subscriptions · Advisory · API · Data licensing · Revenue engine
Critical dependency: Layer 1 (SSI Index data) is the foundation. Layers 2–5 cannot function without it. The Italian SSI v4.0 provides the richest training set; each new country added enriches the neural network and improves transfer-learning accuracy across all countries. The architecture exhibits network effects: the more countries covered, the more valuable each individual country's output becomes.
Python namespace collision avoidance. The implementation (v0.1.0–v0.8.0) uses two non-standard package names to avoid collisions with Python built-in modules:
  • coverage_arch — renamed from coverage to avoid the standard coverage code instrumentation library
  • platform_arch — renamed from platform to avoid the standard platform module that queries OS information
The API and functionality are unchanged; only the import paths differ (e.g., from platform_arch.api_architecture import ...). See §0.8.2 for full details.
Section 0.3

Layer 1 — Data Architecture

Layer 1 is the foundation of the entire SSI-ENN system. It formally specifies the SSI v4.0 Index — its six components, seventeen sub-metrics, normalisation methodology, aggregation rules, and the Gaussian Copula Monte Carlo simulation framework that propagates uncertainty through every downstream calculation. Every number produced by Phases 1–5 originates in Layer 1. This section provides the complete mathematical specification sufficient for implementation, audit, and replication. Version 4.1 extends the mathematical framework with six academic scoring enrichments (§0.3.8), a forward-looking trajectory engine with EU policy cascade (§0.3.9), a BESS risk-space impact model (§0.3.10), a 107-province spatial hierarchy with municipal-level granularity (§0.3.11), a production data pipeline with validation and audit trail (§0.3.12), and a complete implementation status inventory (§0.3.13).

The SSI Index assigns each of Italy's 39,234 distribution substations a multi-dimensional profile across six orthogonal dimensions of grid health. These dimensions were selected to capture the full state space relevant to BESS investment valuation: the quality of supply today (C, V), the physical risk environment (I), the socioeconomic context (E), the DER-driven stress trajectory (S), and the forward-looking transition and climate pathway (T). No single number can describe a substation — the SSI is inherently multivariate, and reducing it to a scalar ranking destroys the information that makes BESS valuation possible.

0.3.1   SSI v4.0 Component Taxonomy

Six components. Twenty sub-metrics. Each sub-metric is independently sourced, validated, and normalised before aggregation into its parent component. The table below is the complete registry.

0.3.2   Sub-Metric Definitions

Each sub-metric is defined with sufficient precision for independent replication. Below are the formal definitions and, where applicable, the computational formulae.

C — Continuity Metrics C1 = SAIDI_j = Σ_i (r_i × N_i) / N_T
j: substation index. i: each interruption event affecting customers served by substation j during the reporting year. r_i: restoration time of event i (minutes). N_i: number of customers affected by event i. N_T: total customers served by substation j. Only sustained interruptions counted (>3 minutes, per ARERA/CEER convention). Excludes exceptional events (force majeure) unless explicitly included by the NRA.

C2 = SAIFI_j = Σ_i N_i / N_T
Same event set as C1. Counts events, not duration.

C3 = MAIFI_j = count of momentary interruptions (≤3 minutes) × N_affected / N_T. Where MAIFI is not reported at substation level (common outside Italy), C3 is estimated from C2 using the CEER-documented ratio MAIFI/SAIFI ≈ 2.5–4.0 depending on network type (overhead vs underground). This ratio is calibrated per territory class using Italian data where both are available.

C4 = Planned outage ratio = planned SAIDI / total SAIDI. Captures the fraction of interruption time due to scheduled maintenance. High C4 indicates either ageing infrastructure requiring frequent maintenance or inadequate live-line working capability.

Data granularity: Italian SAIDI/SAIFI is reported by ARERA at the level of ambito territoriale (territorial zone within each DSO concession area), classified as alta concentrazione (urban, >50K inhabitants), media concentrazione (semi-urban, 5K–50K), and bassa concentrazione (rural, <5K). Each substation is assigned to its territorial zone. This is the finest publicly available resolution. Where DSO open-data initiatives provide feeder-level or substation-level data (E-Distribuzione partial disclosure), the finer data is preferred. For non-Italian countries (Phase 4), CEER Benchmarking Report provides national-level SAIDI/SAIFI; sub-national estimation uses the NN (Phase 3 Stage 2 feature-reduction training).
V — Voltage Quality Metrics V1_j = Σ_k 𝟙[ΔV_k > threshold]
k: each voltage event at the point of common coupling (PCC) of substation j during the reporting year. 𝟙[·]: indicator function (1 if EN 50160 limit is exceeded, 0 otherwise). EN 50160 Clause 4.2.2.2 specifies: 95% of 10-minute mean RMS voltage values in each week must be within ±10% of declared voltage (230V LV, 20kV MV in Italy). V1 counts exceedance events, not the percentage compliance — an exceedance event is a contiguous period where voltage is outside limits.

V2_j = Σ_k (1 − V_residual,k/V_nominal) × d_k
V_residual,k: minimum residual voltage during dip event k (% of nominal). d_k: duration of event k (ms). The product measures the "depth × duration" severity of each dip. This is the standard UNIPEDE/UIE severity measure (equivalent to the area below the ITIC/SEMI F47 curve). V2 aggregates all events in the reporting year. Higher V2 = more severe aggregate PQ degradation.

Note on data sparsity: PQ monitoring is not universally deployed at every Italian substation. Where direct measurement is unavailable, V1 and V2 are estimated from: (a) DSO aggregate PQ compliance reports (ARERA TIQE), (b) network topology proxies (substations at the end of long radial feeders or in areas with high DER penetration exhibit worse PQ — this correlation is quantified in the Italian training data and applied via the NN for sparse-data substations), (c) feeder impedance estimates from I4/I6 topology metrics. The estimation uncertainty is propagated through the Gaussian Copula (§0.3.4) as wider confidence bands on V-derived outputs.
I — Infrastructure Risk Index I_climate,j = {I1_j, I2_j, I3_j} — IRI hazard scores [0–1]
Each climate-hazard IRI follows the standard risk formulation:

IRI_h,j = P(hazard_h | location_j) × Exposure_j × Vulnerability_j

h ∈ {flood, landslide, heat-wave}. P(hazard): annualised probability of a hazard event affecting the substation's geographic location, derived from national hazard maps (ISPRA for Italy, Copernicus EMS for EU). Exposure: fraction of substation assets and feeder km within the hazard zone (GIS intersection of substation service area polygon with hazard-zone polygon). Vulnerability: asset susceptibility factor (function of I5 asset age and construction standard — older assets are more vulnerable to flood/heat; underground cables less vulnerable to wind but more to flood).

I3 (heat-wave) has a distinct formulation because it affects both infrastructure and operations: I3_j = f(HWF, HWI, HWD) where HWF = heat-wave frequency (events/decade from ERA5/CMIP6), HWI = heat-wave intensity (peak daily max temperature relative to local 95th percentile), HWD = heat-wave duration (consecutive days). I3 drives both asset degradation (transformer derating, cable ampacity reduction, BESS calendar-fade acceleration) and human-welfare impacts (W4/W5 community value).

I4_j = count(substations within 5km radius) / π(5)²
Simple density metric from geospatial substation registry. Higher density implies greater redundancy — load can be transferred to adjacent substations during outage. The 5 km radius is calibrated to typical MV feeder reach in Italian distribution networks.

I5_j = IEEE_C57.91_hotspot(T_ambient, load_profile, transformer_rating)
Thermal stress proxy derived from IEEE C57.91 hot-spot temperature model. Combines ambient temperature (from Open-Meteo/ERA5), load profile (from Terna), and transformer thermal characteristics. High I5 indicates substations operating near thermal limits.

I6_j = count(substations within catchment) / area_km²
Substation density (substations per km²). Higher density implies greater redundancy — load can be transferred to adjacent substations during outage. This replaces the previous meshed/radial ratio formulation with a pure density metric aligned with Formula Construct v4.0.2.

I7_j = P_peak,j / P_rated,j
Load stress ratio. P_peak: peak annual load (MW) at substation j from Terna zone-level data disaggregated to substation. P_rated: transformer rated capacity. Ratio approaching 1.0 indicates thermal saturation risk. Source: Terna Open Data Portal + E-Distribuzione transformer ratings.

I8_j = ISO_9223_corrosion_index(PM2.5, NO₂, Cl_deposition, humidity)
Atmospheric corrosion exposure combining air quality (EEA PM2.5/NO₂), coastal chloride deposition (proximity to sea), and time-of-wetness (ERA5 humidity). Normalised to [0,1] from ISO 9223:2012 C1–C5 classification. Source: EEA Air Quality e-Reporting + ISPRA coastal data + ISO 9223 standard.

I9_j = max(flood_score_j, landslide_score_j)
Combined hydrogeological risk. Flood and landslide exposure from ISPRA IdroGEO and ReNDiS/IFFI national inventories. Each component normalised to [0,1] from territorial coverage percentages. Source: ISPRA IdroGEO platform + IFFI landslide inventory.
E — Economic Profile Metrics E1_j = GDP_NUTS3(j) / Population_NUTS3(j)
GDP in purchasing power standard (PPS) at the NUTS-3 provincial level in which substation j is located. Updated annually from Eurostat/ISTAT. Used as contextual variable — does not directly enter valuation but modulates economic multipliers (W3) and informs investor risk appetite.

E2_j = Σ_s (f_s,j × VoLL_s)
s: customer sector (residential, commercial, industrial, critical). f_s,j: fraction of customers at substation j in sector s (from ISTAT census + E-Distribuzione customer-mix data by territorial zone). VoLL_s: sector-specific Value of Lost Load (€/kWh unserved). ARERA publishes Italian VoLL estimates; for cross-country comparisons, CEER methodology is applied with national GDP adjustment. Typical ranges: residential €2–8/kWh, commercial €8–30/kWh, industrial €5–50/kWh (highly sector-dependent), critical (hospitals, data centres) €50–200+/kWh. The customer-mix-weighted E2 at each substation is the economic leverage through which C-component improvements translate into €/year value (D1, W1).
S — DER Saturation Metrics S1_j = P_DER,j / P_transformer,j   (Dimovski ratio)
P_DER,j: total installed DER capacity (kW) connected to the feeders served by substation j. Sourced from GSE Atlaimpianti, which records every incentivised DER installation by connection point. Aggregation to substation level uses the SSI geospatial mapping layer (each DER installation's GPS coordinates mapped to the nearest substation's service area polygon). P_transformer,j: rated apparent power (kVA) of the HV/MV transformer(s) at substation j, from E-Distribuzione asset register.

Critical thresholds: S1 < 0.3 (low stress — DER comfortably within hosting capacity), 0.3–0.7 (moderate — RPF begins occurring at midday peaks in high-solar periods), 0.7–1.0 (high — routine RPF during solar hours, curtailment risk emerging, voltage rise approaching EN 50160 limits), >1.0 (critical — installed DER exceeds transformer rating; RPF is the norm, not the exception; curtailment is active; BESS has maximum congestion-relief value).

S2_j = Σ_h 𝟙[P_generation,j(h) > P_demand,j(h)] / 8,760
h: each hour of the year (8,760 hours). P_generation,j(h): estimated DER generation at hour h (solar from PVGIS irradiance profile × installed PV from GSE; wind from ERA5 wind speed × installed wind capacity). P_demand,j(h): estimated local demand at hour h (ARERA typical load curves by territory class, scaled to transformer nameplate). S2 is the fraction of hours in which generation exceeds demand — i.e., the substation is in net export (reverse power flow). S2 < 0.05 (rare RPF), 0.05–0.15 (seasonal midday RPF), 0.15–0.30 (routine RPF in sunny months), >0.30 (structural RPF — the substation is more generator than consumer for a significant fraction of the year).

S3_j ∈ {0, 0.5, 1.0} — categorical criticality classification. 0 = non-critical loads only. 0.5 = hospital or transport infrastructure served. 1.0 = multiple critical facilities served. Source: Min. Salute Health Registry; ARERA critical-load classification. Where healthcare facility density data is available, S3 is overridden with a continuous score. S3 is fixed categorical with zero Monte Carlo sampling uncertainty.
T — Energy Transition Exposure UPDATED v4.0.2 T1 = α_DER × N(DER_ratio) + α_VAR × N(DER_variability) + α_EV × N(EV_load_ratio)
DER_ratio(j) = Σ DER_capacity_municipality / peak_load_municipality. Source: GSE Atlaimpianti (DER capacity registry) + Terna Open Data (load by zone). Annual. Municipality-level aggregated to substation catchment.

DER_variability(j) = σ(weekly_DER_output) / μ(weekly_DER_output). Source: ENTSO-E Transparency Platform (generation by type, zone level). Coefficient of variation measures output intermittency.

EV_load_ratio(j) = (EV_count_municipality × avg_kW_per_EV) / transformer_capacity. Source: ENEA RAEE (EV registry by province) × 7.4 kW avg charger / existing transformer capacity. Annual.

Sub-metric weights: α_DER = 0.50 · α_VAR = 0.30 · α_EV = 0.20

Interpretation: High T1 → substation faces significant energy-transition stress: high DER penetration relative to load (reverse power flow risk, protection miscoordination, voltage rise) AND/OR high EV charging burden on transformer capacity. T1 intentionally captures transition stress, not DER benefit.

Note: Climate trajectory (CMIP6 SSP2-4.5) now enters via the R2 adaptive IRI modifier (§0.3.7 below), not as a separate T2 sub-metric. This aligns with SSI Formula Construct v4.0.2 where climate trajectory modifies the I-component through the modifier chain rather than as a standalone metric.

0.3.3   Normalisation & Scoring

Raw sub-metrics span heterogeneous units (minutes, ratios, €/kWh, scores). Before aggregation into component scores and propagation through the copula, each sub-metric is normalised to a common [0, 1] scale using the following methodology:

Normalisation Protocol x̃_m,j = F_m(x_m,j)   where F_m is the empirical CDF of metric m across all j ∈ {1, ..., 39,234}
Step 1 — Empirical CDF transform. Each raw sub-metric value x_m,j is transformed to its percentile rank within the Italian population of 39,234 substations. This produces a uniform [0, 1] distribution for every metric, regardless of the original distribution shape (which may be highly skewed — SAIDI is right-skewed with fat tails; Dimovski ratios are approximately log-normal; IRI scores are zero-inflated). The CDF transform is the standard first step in copula modelling (Sklar's theorem) and simultaneously achieves normalisation and distributional standardisation.

Step 2 — Polarity alignment. For metrics where higher raw values indicate worse grid health (C1, C2, C3, C4, V1, V2, I1, I2, I3, I5, I7, I8, I9, S1, S2, S3, T1), the normalised score is used as-is: x̃ close to 1 = high stress. For metrics where higher raw values indicate better grid health (I4, I6), the score is inverted: x̃' = 1 − x̃, so that 1 consistently means "most stressed / most in need of BESS intervention" across all metrics. E1 and E2 are contextual — not inverted, but flagged as modulating rather than directional.

Step 3 — Component aggregation. Each component score is the weighted average of its constituent normalised sub-metrics:
Component Aggregation SSI_C,j = w_C1 · x̃_C1,j + w_C2 · x̃_C2,j + w_C3 · x̃_C3,j   where Σw = 1
Analogous formulae for V, I, E, S, T. The weights within each component are:

C: w_C1 = 0.40, w_C2 = 0.30, w_C3 = 0.15, w_C4 = 0.15. Duration (C1/SAIDI) weighted highest because it drives economic loss (VoLL × duration); frequency (C2/SAIFI) captures disruption count; MT exceed rate (C3) and planned outages (C4) each at 0.15.

V: V uses a single severity-weighted composite: V = N(V1_total × (1 + γ × V2_severe_ratio)), γ=0.50. V1 frequency and V2 severity are combined into a single metric with weight 1.00, matching SSI Formula Construct v4.0.2.

I: w_I1 = 0.12, w_I2 = 0.09, w_I3 = 0.15, w_I4 = 0.12, w_I5 = 0.12, w_I6 = 0.12, w_I7 = 0.10, w_I8 = 0.08, w_I9 = 0.10. Heat-wave (I3) weighted highest (broadest impact + steepest CMIP6 escalation). I1/I4/I5/I6 at 0.12 each. New I7 (load stress), I8 (corrosion), I9 (hydrogeological) capture physical environment dimensions from v4.0.2. I1–I3 weights are adaptive via R2 modifier; I5–I9 pass through at base weights.

E: w_E1 = 0.55, w_E2 = 0.45. Penalties (E1) weighted higher per SSI v4.0.2 calibration; E2 productivity enriched with innovation composite (HRST + startup density) providing up to +10% boost.

S: w_S1 = 0.75, w_S2 = 0.125, w_S3 = 0.125. S component weight reduced from 0.25 → 0.20 in v4.0.2 (Sobol analysis: S2/S3 contribute zero MC variance as categorical variables). S1 intra-weight raised to 0.75. S2/S3 are fixed categorical with zero Monte Carlo sampling uncertainty — deterministic fraction reduced to 5.0%.

T: T1 is a single composite metric with weight 1.00. Its internal sub-metric weights are α_DER = 0.50, α_VAR = 0.30, α_EV = 0.20 (see §0.3.2). σ_total ≈ 28%.

Top-level component weights (R_base): w_C = 0.30, w_V = 0.10, w_I = 0.25, w_E = 0.10, w_S = 0.20, w_T = 0.05. Sum = 1.00. R_base = 0.30·C + 0.10·V + 0.25·I + 0.10·E + 0.20·S + 0.05·T.

Weight calibration: Weights are calibrated by Sobol global sensitivity analysis on Phase 1+2 outputs: which sub-metrics explain the most variance in final BESS valuations? Initial weights are set by expert elicitation; Sobol analysis (using the 10,000-iteration Monte Carlo) identifies sub-metrics that contribute disproportionately to valuation variance and adjusts weights iteratively until the SSI component structure is maximally informative for downstream investment decisions. The weights above are the current calibration from Italian data. They will be reviewed per-country as Phase 4 expands the dataset.

Alternative weighting methodology (§0.3.8): In addition to expert-elicited weights, Layer 1 now implements Shannon entropy-based objective weighting as a data-driven complement. Entropy weights are derived from the information content (dispersion) of each metric across the fleet — metrics with higher discriminating power receive higher weight. A hybrid AHP-Entropy approach blends expert weights (60%) with entropy-derived weights (40%), providing an auditable, data-driven justification for weight evolution as fleet data grows. See §0.3.8.1 for full specification.

0.3.4   Gaussian Copula Monte Carlo

The SSI components are not independent — a substation with poor continuity (high C) often also has poor voltage quality (high V), aged infrastructure (high I5), and is located in a rural area with high DER penetration (high S) but low economic profile (low E). Ignoring these dependencies would produce incorrect joint probability distributions and therefore incorrect valuations. The Gaussian Copula provides the mathematically rigorous framework for modelling multivariate dependencies while allowing each marginal distribution to take its natural (non-Gaussian) shape.

Copula Construction (Sklar's Theorem) F(x_1, ..., x_20) = C(F_1(x_1), ..., F_20(x_20); Σ)
Sklar's Theorem: any joint distribution F of 20 random variables can be decomposed into its marginal distributions F_m (one per sub-metric) and a copula function C that captures the dependence structure. The Gaussian Copula uses a multivariate normal dependence structure parameterised by correlation matrix Σ ∈ ℝ^{20×20}.

Construction steps:
(1) Marginal modelling. Each sub-metric's distribution across 39,234 substations is modelled non-parametrically (empirical CDF, as in §0.3.3 Step 1). No normality assumption is imposed on the marginals — SAIDI can be Pareto-tailed, Dimovski ratios can be log-normal, IRI scores can be zero-inflated. This flexibility is the copula's key advantage over multivariate normal modelling.

(2) Uniform transform. x̃_m,j = F_m(x_m,j) maps each sub-metric to U[0,1]. This is the normalised score from §0.3.3.

(3) Gaussian transform. z_m,j = Φ⁻¹(x̃_m,j) maps each uniform marginal to a standard normal marginal via the inverse normal CDF Φ⁻¹. The vector z_j = (z_1,j, ..., z_20,j) is now approximately multivariate normal with mean 0 and correlation matrix Σ.

(4) Correlation estimation. Σ is estimated from the 39,234 × 20 matrix of z-scores using Kendall's tau rank correlation (more robust to outliers than Pearson) converted to Pearson correlation via the relationship ρ = sin(π·τ/2). The resulting Σ is validated for positive-definiteness (nearest positive-definite projection applied if needed).
Monte Carlo Simulation Engine For k = 1, ..., 10,000:   z^(k) ~ N(0, Σ) → ũ^(k) = Φ(z^(k)) → x^(k) = F⁻¹(ũ^(k))
Simulation protocol:
(1) Draw z^(k) from the 20-dimensional multivariate normal distribution N(0, Σ) using Cholesky decomposition of Σ (L·L^T = Σ; z^(k) = L · ε^(k) where ε^(k) ~ N(0, I)).
(2) Transform to uniform marginals: ũ_m^(k) = Φ(z_m^(k)).
(3) Transform to original scales via inverse empirical CDFs: x_m^(k) = F_m⁻¹(ũ_m^(k)). The vector x^(k) = (x_1^(k), ..., x_20^(k)) is a simulated substation profile that respects the observed marginal distributions AND inter-metric correlations.
(4) Pass x^(k) through the full Phase 1+2 valuation engine (D1–D5, W1–W5, R1–R11, Options A–F) to produce valuation sample V^(k).
(5) After 10,000 iterations: V = {V^(1), ..., V^(10,000)} is the valuation distribution for this substation. Extract P10, P50 (median), P90, mean, and standard deviation. This is the uncertainty-quantified investment-grade output.

10,000 iterations is sufficient for convergence of P10/P90 to within ±2% (validated by bootstrap resampling). Run-time: ~0.5s per substation on modern GPU; full Italian scan (39,234 substations × 2 configs) ≈ 11 hours. Incremental country scans are faster because the copula Σ is pre-computed.
Tail-Dependent Copula Extension C_tail = (1 − α) · C_Gaussian(Σ) + α · C_Clayton(θ)   α calibrated from tail observations
The standard Gaussian Copula has zero tail dependence: the probability that two variables are simultaneously extreme does not increase in the tails. This is unrealistic for grid infrastructure, where extreme events cluster — a severe heat-wave (I3 tail) simultaneously causes SAIDI spikes (C1 tail), voltage instability (V1/V2 tail), and DER curtailment (S2 tail). To capture this, the copula is augmented with a Clayton component in the lower tail (where "lower" = "worse" in polarity-aligned metrics).

The Clayton copula C_Clayton(u_1, ..., u_n; θ) = (Σ u_i^{−θ} − n + 1)^{−1/θ} has lower-tail dependence coefficient λ_L = 2^{−1/θ} > 0 for θ > 0. The mixing weight α is calibrated from Italian historical data: during extreme events (defined as days where C1 > 95th percentile), what is the conditional probability that V, I, and S simultaneously exceed their respective 90th percentiles? This observed joint-tail probability determines α and θ.

Why this matters for BESS valuation: Tail dependence is precisely where BESS value is highest — during simultaneous grid stress across multiple dimensions, the BESS is most valuable and the community impact of inaction is most severe. A Gaussian-only copula underestimates both the frequency and severity of multi-dimensional stress events, systematically undervaluing W4 (resilience insurance), W5 (communications cascade), R11 (CRS), and Options D (abandon floor) and F (CRS emergence). The tail-dependent copula corrects this bias, producing wider P10–P90 bands and higher tail-risk-weighted expected values — the financially conservative adjustment for investment-grade analysis.

Full SSI component coverage in option valuation: The data flow matrix reveals that the Real Options framework (Phase 2 §2.3) now captures value across the complete SSI component space. Options A and E are driven primarily by S/T (DER trajectory → investment timing). Option C is driven by degradation × I3/T2 (climate-path Bayesian update at repowering). Option D's floor is set by I/T/C (climate hazard → W5/R11 resilience value). Option F captures three orthogonal dimensions: S/T → LMP, V → PQaaS, I/T/C → CRS. No SSI component is wasted — every dimension of grid health contributes to at least one valuation output, and most contribute to multiple outputs through different causal channels. This is the architectural property that makes the SSI a sufficient statistic for BESS investment valuation: if you know the SSI profile, you know enough to price the asset.

0.3.8   Academic Scoring Frameworks NEW — v4.1 Academic Enrichment

Six peer-reviewed methodological enrichments address known limitations of the additive weighting approach and strengthen the Layer 1 scoring framework. Each concept is implemented in academic.py and produces parallel scoring outputs alongside the baseline R_base. The combined academic score (R_combined) is systematically 5–10% higher than R_base, indicating that the linear additive formula under-estimates composite risk — particularly for substations with correlated degradation profiles.

0.3.8.1   Shannon Entropy Objective Weighting

Reference: Shannon, C. E. (1948). A Mathematical Theory of Communication. Bell System Technical Journal, 27(3), 379–423.

Shannon Entropy Weight Derivation H_j = −(1/ln n) × Σ_i p_{ij} · ln(p_{ij})    d_j = 1 − H_j    w_j^{entropy} = d_j / Σ_k d_k
p_{ij} = x_{ij} / Σ_i x_{ij} is the proportion of metric j's total value carried by substation i. ε = 10⁻¹⁰ added to avoid log(0).

Hybrid AHP-Entropy: w_j^{hybrid} = α × w_j^{expert} + (1−α) × w_j^{entropy}, α = 0.60 (60% expert, 40% data-driven), normalised to Σw = 1.0.

Finding: Entropy shifts ~14% of weight from C (0.30 → 0.18) to I (0.25 → 0.39). This is expected — V uses default values (zero entropy), while I sub-metrics vary substantially across regions. As real data populates V and E, entropy weights converge toward expert weights. The divergence is itself a data-quality diagnostic: large entropy-expert gaps flag components where fleet data is insufficiently discriminating.

KL divergence: D_KL(w^{expert} || w^{entropy}) measures the information-theoretic distance between the two weighting schemes. Tracked per data refresh to quantify weight convergence.

0.3.8.2   DSRR Multiplicative Aggregation

Reference: Bruneau, M. et al. (2003). A Framework to Quantitatively Assess and Enhance the Seismic Resilience of Communities. Earthquake Spectra, 19(4), 733–752.

DSRR Multiplicative Risk Model R_{DSRR} = D^α × S^β × (1−R)^γ × (1−Rec)^δ   where α+β+γ+δ = 1
Danger (D): T-component (transition stress — external stressor intensity).
Susceptibility (S): 0.65·C + 0.35·V (exposure to disruption).
Resistance (R): I-component (physical condition — inverted: 1−I = resistance capacity).
Recoverability (Rec): 0.45·E + 0.55·S (bounce-back capacity — inverted).

Exponents: α=0.15, β=0.35, γ=0.30, δ=0.20 (calibrated to preserve rank-order with additive R_base). ε=0.05 floor on all factors to prevent zero-product collapse.

Finding: DSRR gap |R_mult − R_additive| reveals low-R_base regions with hidden compounding vulnerability: Piemonte and Valle d'Aosta show a 52% gap ratio, meaning the multiplicative formula assigns materially higher risk than the linear one. This captures the nonlinearity thesis from the Altinium research paper on infrastructure price×volume — threshold interactions that linear weighting misses.

Role: Diagnostic alternative, not replacement. DSRR gap > 0.10 triggers a flag in the audit report.

0.3.8.3   Lognormal Fragility Curves

Reference: Rossetto, T. & Elnashai, A. S. (2003). Derivation of vulnerability functions for European-type RC structures. Journal of Earthquake Engineering, 7(sup001), 89–106.

Fragility Function P(damage | x) = Φ[ (ln(x) − ln(μ)) / β ]
Φ = standard normal CDF. μ = median stressor intensity at 50% damage probability. β = dispersion (log-standard deviation).

Replaces linear normalisation (Method C) for climate and seismic stressors where physical behaviour is genuinely non-linear. Pre-calibrated parameters:

Thermal hotspot: μ = 65°C, β = 0.30 (transformer winding limit; failure onset ~50°C)
Seismic PGA: μ = 0.20g, β = 0.50 (zone 2/1 boundary; substation anchorage failure)
Flood depth: μ = 0.50m, β = 0.40 (equipment room inundation threshold)
Heatwave days: μ = 5 days, β = 0.35 (thermal derating cumulation >5 consecutive days >40°C)
PM2.5: μ = 35 μg/m³, β = 0.25 (WHO limit; accelerated corrosion onset)
Wind speed: μ = 30 m/s, β = 0.30 (overhead line mechanical damage threshold)

Finding: Fragility normalisation produces scores 15–30% higher than linear (Method C) at threshold-proximate stressor levels. Recommended for adoption when calibration data (manufacturer failure curves) becomes available. Currently diagnostic — runs in parallel with Method C.

0.3.8.4   Enhanced Network Centrality

Reference: Buldyrev, S. V. et al. (2010). Catastrophic cascade of failures in interdependent networks. Nature, 464(7291), 1025–1028.

Enhanced R4 — Five-Factor Topology Scoring R4_enh = clip( base(degree) × (1 + 0.10·BC + 0.15·bridge + 0.05·(1−CC) + 0.08·EV), 0.80, 1.40 )
BC = betweenness centrality percentile (Brandes' algorithm, O(VE)).
bridge = topological bridge detection (Tarjan's algorithm).
CC = clustering coefficient (local triangle density — low CC = isolated node).
EV = eigenvector centrality percentile (power iteration — influence metric).
Spectral gap λ₂/λ₁ = graph-level resilience diagnostic (low = fragile graph).

All algorithms implemented from scratch (no networkx dependency). Requires adjacency data from grid topology graph. Community detection via label propagation identifies sub-graph clusters.

Status: Algorithm ready; activates when Terna/e-distribuzione network topology data is ingested. Extends R4 range from [0.80, 1.35] to [0.80, 1.40].

0.3.8.5   Cyber-Physical Cross-Component Interaction (CPIndex)

Reference: Cyber-physical systems scoring literature (NIST Framework, IEC 62443).

Cross-Component Interaction Term CP_interaction = Σ_{(i,j) ∈ pairs} ρ_{ij} × score_i × score_j    R_{base,cp} = R_base + λ_cp × CP_interaction
Interaction pairs and their correlation strengths:

I5_SCADA × I_physical (ρ=0.40): cyber vulnerability amplifies physical failure risk
I8_firmware × C_continuity (ρ=0.30): old firmware → more frequent/longer outages
I7_load × S1_DER (ρ=0.35): load stress × DER saturation → voltage instability
I3_heatwave × I5_thermal (ρ=0.45): climate + thermal derating = compounding degradation
T1_transition × V_voltage (ρ=0.25): DER transition → voltage quality deterioration

λ_cp = coupling strength [0, 1], default 1.0. Phase-in parameter for production deployment.

Finding: CPIndex adds +3–9% to R_base. Sicilia (+9.1%) and Lombardia (+8.1%) show the highest cross-component amplification. This is the strongest single academic correction, indicating that the additive formula materially under-estimates risk for substations with correlated degradation profiles. The interaction term is additive to R_base, not multiplicative — it captures second-order effects missed by the linear weighted sum.

0.3.8.6   Jensen's Inequality Correction

Reference: Jensen, J. L. W. V. (1906). Sur les fonctions convexes et les inégalités entre les valeurs moyennes. Acta Mathematica, 30(1), 175–193.

Convexity Correction for Aggregation Bias Jensen_corr = (κ−1) / (2κ) × Var_w(comp_k)    R_{base,J} = R_base + Jensen_corr
Var_w = weighted variance of component scores: Var = Σ w_k × (comp_k − R_base)².
κ = 2 (quadratic cost function; infrastructure damage costs are at minimum quadratic in severity).

When the true cost function f(R) is convex, E[f(X)] > f(E[X]). The additive R_base = f(E[X]) systematically under-estimates true risk. A substation scoring 0.8 on one component and 0.2 on another is riskier than one scoring 0.5 on both — even though R_base is identical. The Jensen correction penalises this dispersion.

Finding: Jensen correction adds +0.001–0.003 per substation. Numerically modest, but correctly identifies substations with highly unbalanced risk profiles. Becomes material when combined with CPIndex.

0.3.8.7   Combined Academic Score

R_combined — Academic-Enriched Baseline R_combined = R_base + (R_entropy − R_base) + Jensen_corr + CP_interaction
Applies entropy weighting, Jensen correction, and CPIndex simultaneously. DSRR and fragility curves remain diagnostic alternatives (not additive).

Net effect: R_combined is systematically 5–10% higher than R_base. This gap narrows as real data replaces defaults (entropy weights converge toward expert weights), but CPIndex and Jensen terms persist as structural corrections that the additive formula cannot capture by construction.
Production integration: R_combined runs in parallel with R_base — it does not replace the baseline. The delta (R_combined − R_base) is reported as "academic uplift" in the audit trail and serves as a risk-underestimation warning. When the delta exceeds 0.05 for a given substation, the audit report flags it for review. The λ_cp coupling parameter (§0.3.8.5) provides a production dial to phase in CPIndex gradually.

0.3.9   Forward-Looking Trajectory Engine NEW — v4.1

The trajectory engine projects the SSI metric space forward along six stress vectors aligned to EU policy milestones (PNIEC 2030, 2035, 2050). Implemented in trajectory.py, it transforms the SSI from a point-in-time snapshot into a trajectory-aware risk assessment.

Six Stress Vectors x_m(t) = x_m(t₀) × (1 + Δ_m(t)) + ε_m(t)
Vector 1 — DER Penetration (S1, T1): PNIEC targets: solar 30→80→150 GW (2024→2030→2050), wind 13→23→60 GW. Projects S1 Dimovski ratio and T1 DER stress along these trajectories. Regional variation from GSE installation rates.

Vector 2 — Harmonic & Non-Linear Load (V1, V2): EV charging (3M→10M stock by 2030→2050) and heat pump deployment drive harmonic injection. Projects V-component degradation from power electronics penetration.

Vector 3 — Climate (I1, I2, I3): CMIP6 SSP2-4.5 regional deltas: North +8%, Centre +12%, South +18%, Islands +20% increase in I3 heat-wave by 2040. Flood and landslide frequencies from ISPRA trend extrapolation.

Vector 4 — Cyber Exposure (R7): NIS2 directive timeline: mandatory compliance by 2026, full enforcement by 2028. Projects cyber exposure reduction trajectory.

Vector 5 — Grid Congestion (I7): Load growth from electrification (transport, heating) vs network reinforcement lag. Projects I7 load stress trajectory.

Vector 6 — Socio-Economic Resilience (E1, E2): Regional GDP convergence projections; VoLL escalation with increasing digital dependency.

Correlation matrix evolution: Correlation between T1↔V and T1↔S1 strengthens with DER growth (ρ increases 20–40% by 2030). The trajectory engine evolves the MC correlation matrix Ω(t) alongside the metric projections.
Stress Acceleration a_stress(t) = (1/K) Σ_k [ (x_k(t) − x_k(t₀)) / x_k(t₀) ] × w_k
Scalar acceleration metric indicating how fast the risk environment is deteriorating. A substation with a_stress > 0.15 is experiencing rapid environmental degradation — BESS deployment becomes time-critical (the value of delay is negative). Reported alongside the static SSI score in the audit output.
PNIEC alignment: All trajectory endpoints are calibrated to Italy's Piano Nazionale Integrato per l'Energia e il Clima. Midpoint targets (2030) are the primary planning horizon; 2035 and 2050 endpoints provide long-term sensitivity. The trajectory engine outputs can be fed directly into the Real Options framework (§0.4.5) to compute time-dependent option exercise boundaries.

0.3.9.1   EU Policy Cascade NEW — v4.1.1

Five enhancements to the trajectory engine that propagate EU-wide regulatory instruments down to substation-level impact, ensuring the SSI's forward-looking projections reflect the full policy stack — not just PNIEC targets but also the Electricity Market Design Reform, RED III, EPBD recast, and CBAM border adjustment mechanism.

EU Policy Cascade — Five Enhancement Layers x_m(t) = x_m(t₀) × (1 + Δ_m(t)) × Π_cascade(t) + ε_m(t)
Layer 1 — EMD Reform Revenue Impact: The EU Electricity Market Design Reform (Regulation 2024/1747) introduces long-term Power Purchase Agreements (PPAs), Contracts for Difference (CfDs), and enhanced ancillary service markets. The cascade applies revenue-stack multipliers to R1–R11 trajectories: CfD availability increases R3 (frequency response) certainty by 15–25%; PPA framework stabilises arbitrage revenue (R7) volatility by 10–20%. Implementation: apply_emd_reform_impact() in trajectory.py.

Layer 2 — RED III DER Acceleration: Renewable Energy Directive III (Directive 2023/2413) raises the 2030 RE target to 42.5% (with 45% aspiration). This accelerates the S1 (Dimovski ratio) and T1 (DER stress) trajectories beyond national PNIEC assumptions. The cascade applies a DER acceleration multiplier: 1.08–1.15× for Italy (already above-trend), 1.15–1.30× for lagging member states. Projects faster reverse-power-flow stress and voltage-quality degradation at DER-saturated substations.

Layer 3 — EPBD Recast Heat Pump Cascade: The Energy Performance of Buildings Directive recast (Directive 2024/1275) mandates phase-out of fossil heating in new buildings (2030) and existing buildings (2040). This drives massive heat-pump electrification: +8–15 TWh additional Italian demand by 2035. The cascade modifies I7 (load stress) trajectories with regional heat-pump adoption curves derived from building stock age profiles (ISTAT Censimento). Winter evening peaks increase transformer loading by 12–25% in poorly insulated building stock areas (southern Italy, pre-1970 buildings).

Layer 4 — CBAM Industrial Electrification: The Carbon Border Adjustment Mechanism (Regulation 2023/956) incentivises energy-intensive industries to electrify to avoid border carbon tariffs. The cascade identifies substations serving industrial zones (from ISTAT SLL — Sistemi Locali del Lavoro) and applies industrial-electrification load growth multipliers to I7 trajectories: +5–15% additional peak demand in steel/cement/chemical clusters by 2030–2035.

Layer 5 — Cross-Policy Compounding: The four cascades above are not independent — they compound. A substation in a high-DER, heat-pump-heavy, industrial zone experiences the simultaneous stress of all four policy vectors. The cross-policy compounding function applies a non-linear interaction term: Π_cascade = Π_i(1 + δ_i) × (1 + λ_cross × Σ_{i≠j} δ_i × δ_j) where λ_cross ≈ 0.15 captures the super-additive stress from concurrent electrification pathways.
Implementation: The EU policy cascade is implemented in trajectory.py as five composable functions: apply_emd_reform_impact(), apply_red3_der_acceleration(), apply_epbd_heat_pump_cascade(), apply_cbam_industrial_electrification(), and apply_cross_policy_compounding(). Each function is independently toggleable via configuration flags, enabling scenario analysis with partial policy implementation (e.g., "what if CBAM is delayed?"). The cascade is applied after the base trajectory projection and before the Monte Carlo correlation matrix evolution. Committed in ssi-layer1 v4.1.1 (commit c7b0724).

0.3.10   BESS Impact — Dual Score Architecture NEW — v4.1

While Layer 2 (§0.4) handles BESS valuation in monetary terms, Layer 1 now provides a risk-space BESS impact assessment: how much does a BESS reduce the SSI score at a given substation? This dual-score architecture (SSI_base → SSI_mitigated) quantifies the grid-health improvement from BESS deployment directly in SSI units, before any monetary conversion. Implemented in bess_impact.py.

BESS Dual Score Architecture SSI_mitigated = R_base × Π_k (1 − δ_k) × (1 − synergy)    BESS_value = SSI_base − SSI_mitigated
Reference BESS: 4.99 MW / 19.96 MWh + 1 MW compute, 150ms response, islanding capable.

Component-level mitigation (δ_k):
δ_C (Continuity): Islanding + fast response reduces SAIDI/SAIFI. δ_C = 0.15–0.35 depending on outage profile.
δ_V (Voltage): Active power factor correction stabilises PCC voltage. δ_V = 0.10–0.25.
δ_I (Infrastructure): Thermal derating reduction from peak shaving. δ_I = 0.05–0.15.
δ_E (Economic): VoLL reduction from improved reliability. δ_E = 0.08–0.20.
δ_S (Saturation): Absorbs reverse power flow at DER-saturated substations. δ_S = 0.20–0.40.
δ_T (Transition): Smooths DER variability. δ_T = 0.10–0.20.

Modifier adjustments:
R4 (topology): BESS with islanding capability reduces topological vulnerability at radial endpoints.
R6 (restoration): BESS enables sub-second restoration, reducing CAIDI to near-zero for stored-energy events.

Synergy (super-additivity): Jensen's inequality applied to mitigation — compound risk reduction exceeds the sum of parts. Synergy = (κ−1)/(2κ) × Var(δ_k), typically +2–5% additional reduction.

Value signal (inverted-U): BESS_value = f(SSI) follows an inverted-U: low SSI (healthy grid) → marginal value; medium SSI (0.35–0.65) → sweet spot; high SSI (critical) → saturates. The value signal identifies the deployment priority: substations where BESS has the highest SSI-space impact per MW deployed.
Investment decision integration: The BESS impact dual score provides a risk-space complement to the monetary valuation in Layer 2 (§0.4). A substation may have high NPV (attractive for investors) but low SSI impact (marginal grid benefit), or vice versa. The dual-score architecture enables Altinium to offer both financial and grid-health perspectives to different stakeholders: investors (NPV-optimised deployment), DNOs (SSI-impact-optimised deployment), and regulators (combined Pareto frontier). The fleet-level rank_deployment_priority() function produces a ranked portfolio ordered by BESS value signal.

0.3.11   Provincial & Municipal Spatial Granularity NEW — v4.1.2

The SSI v4.0 operates at regional level (20 Italian regions) for most data sources, which masks significant intra-regional variation. Version 4.1.2 introduces a complete 107-province spatial hierarchy bridging the gap between regional data and substation-level scoring. This enables provincial-level climate resolution, socioeconomic disaggregation, and continuity estimation — producing materially different SSI scores for substations within the same region but in different provinces.

Spatial Hierarchy Italy: 20 Regions → 107 Provinces → ~7,900 Municipalities → ~39,234 MV Substations
Each province is mapped to its ISTAT code, NUTS3 identifier, parent region, macro-region (north/centre/south/islands), ARERA territory class (alta_concentrazione/media_concentrazione/bassa_concentrazione), seismic zone (1–4), and climate zone (A–F). The lookup tables are implemented in spatial.py with 107 entries validated against ISTAT's official province registry.

Name normalisation: Italian province names vary across data sources — ISTAT uses "Reggio nell'Emilia", ARERA uses "Reggio Emilia", GSE uses "RE". A canonical name normalisation layer (normalize_province_name()) maps all known aliases to a single canonical form, ensuring consistent joins across ARERA, GSE, ISTAT, and ISPRA data sources. The alias dictionary currently covers 15 variant spellings (e.g., "Monza e Brianza" ↔ "Monza e della Brianza", "Reggio Calabria" ↔ "Reggio di Calabria").

0.3.11.1   Provincial Downscaling Methodology

Where data is available only at regional level (e.g., ARERA SAIDI/SAIFI by region), the downscaling engine distributes regional values to provinces using four weighting strategies, selected per metric based on the underlying data-generating process:

Four Downscaling Strategies x_province = x_region × w_province / Σ_p∈region w_p
Population weighting (w = population): Used for demand-correlated metrics — GDP per capita, VoLL, energy poverty index, customer count. Rationale: economic activity scales with population.

Area weighting (w = area_km²): Used for spatially distributed hazards — flood exposure, landslide coverage, heatwave intensity. Rationale: natural hazards correlate with geographic extent.

Uniform weighting (w = 1): Used for regulatory metrics — SAIDI/SAIFI territory class multipliers, cyber exposure scores. Rationale: regulatory parameters apply uniformly within their jurisdiction.

Density weighting (w = population/area): Used for infrastructure stress metrics — transformer loading, substation density, DER concentration. Rationale: infrastructure stress concentrates in dense areas.

Territory class correction: After downscaling, a territory-class multiplier refines the estimate. ARERA classifies substations into three territory classes with materially different reliability profiles: alta concentrazione (urban, >50K inhabitants, multiplier 0.65×), media concentrazione (semi-urban, 5K–50K, multiplier 1.00×), and bassa concentrazione (rural, <5K, multiplier 1.45×). These multipliers reflect the empirical SAIDI ratio between territory classes from ARERA's annual quality reports.

0.3.11.2   Provincial Climate Resolution

The trajectory engine's climate projections (§0.3.9, Vector 3) originally used four macro-region deltas (North/Centre/South/Islands). Provincial resolution refines this to 107 province-level climate deltas by combining macro-region base deltas with provincial hazard risk classifications:

Provincial Climate Delta Δ_climate,province(t) = Δ_macro(t) × (1 + β × (risk_class − 2) / 3)
Δ_macro(t): Base climate delta from CMIP6 SSP2-4.5 for the province's macro-region (North +8%, Centre +12%, South +18%, Islands +20% by 2040).

risk_class: Province-level flood risk class (1–4) or landslide risk class (1–4) from ISPRA IdroGEO, depending on hazard type. Class 4 = highest risk.

β = 0.15: Sensitivity parameter calibrated to historical damage data. A province with risk_class = 4 (highest) receives a 10% uplift above its macro-region delta; risk_class = 1 (lowest) receives a 5% reduction. This produces meaningful provincial variation: e.g., within the "South" macro-region, Cosenza (high landslide risk, class 4) gets a higher climate delta than Bari (low risk, class 1).

Fallback: If provincial risk data is unavailable, the macro-region delta is used unchanged (β = 0). The get_provincial_climate_delta() function in trajectory.py handles the resolution cascade: province → macro-region → national default.

0.3.11.3   Provincial Reference Datasets

Four provincial-level reference datasets are compiled in provincial_data.py, covering all 107 Italian provinces with validated data from ISTAT, ARERA, GSE, and ISPRA sources:

DatasetFields per ProvincePrimary SourceUpdate Frequency
SocioeconomicGDP per capita, population, unemployment rate, energy poverty index, elderly ratio, building age ratioISTAT BES, Eurostat NUTS3Annual
DER PenetrationFV installed MW, FV installation count, wind installed MW, DER ratio (S1), biogas MWGSE Atlaimpianti, AtlasoleQuarterly
ContinuitySAIDI, SAIFI, MAIFI, territory class, planned outage ratio, CAIDIARERA TIQE annual reportsAnnual
Climate ExposureFlood risk class, landslide risk class, heatwave exposure, seismic PGA, wildfire risk classISPRA IdroGEO, INGV seismic hazardBiennial
Implementation: The provincial spatial hierarchy is implemented across two new modules: spatial.py (~55 KB, 107-province lookup tables, downscaling functions, name normalisation) and provincial_data.py (~65 KB, four provincial reference datasets with getter functions). The schemas.py SubstationInput model is extended with eight new municipal fields (codice_comune, comune_name, codice_provincia, municipality_population, municipality_area_km2, municipality_altitude_m, municipality_density, macro_region). 106 new tests validate the spatial hierarchy. Committed in ssi-layer1 v1.2.0 (commit 2e5cb8f).

0.3.12   Real Data Pipeline Architecture NEW — v4.1.3

Production deployment requires the transition from hardcoded reference datasets (acceptable for development and validation) to a robust data pipeline that can ingest real source files (ARERA CSV, GSE Atlasole exports, ISTAT BES tables, ISPRA shapefiles), validate them against expected schemas, detect anomalies, and maintain a complete audit trail for regulatory compliance. Three new modules implement this pipeline.

0.3.12.1   Data Loader — Unified Ingestion with Fallback

DataLoader — File-First / Hardcoded-Fallback Strategy data(source) = file_load(source) if exists(file_path) else hardcoded_fallback(source)
The DataLoader class in data_loader.py implements a unified loading interface that first attempts to read from external files (CSV, JSON, YAML) in the data/ directory, and falls back to the hardcoded Python dictionaries when files are not available. This preserves backward compatibility — the system works identically to before when no external files are present, but switches to real data automatically when files are placed in the expected locations.

File map: Each data source maps to an expected file path: data/arera_tiqe_2024.csv (ARERA SAIDI/SAIFI), data/gse_atlasole.csv (GSE solar installations), data/istat_bes.csv (ISTAT wellbeing indicators), data/ispra_climate.csv (ISPRA climate exposure), data/regional_socioeconomic.csv, data/regional_der.csv, data/regional_caidi.csv, data/regional_gwh.csv.

Source manifest: The DataLoader tracks every loaded source in a manifest recording: source name, origin ("file" or "hardcoded"), file path (if applicable), SHA256 file hash, file modification time, and row count. This manifest is exposed via get_manifest() and can be attached to the Layer1Output for downstream audit.

Completeness reporting: get_data_completeness() returns a percentage indicating how much of the data pipeline is running on real files vs hardcoded fallbacks. Target: 100% file-based before production deployment.

0.3.12.2   Data Validator — Multi-Layer Validation

DataValidator — Validation Layers ValidationReport = { valid: bool, errors: [], warnings: [], out_of_range: [] }
The DataValidator class in data_validator.py applies five layers of validation to every data source before it enters the SSI computation pipeline:

Layer 1 — Range validation: Every numeric field is checked against physically plausible bounds defined in config.py VALIDATION_RANGES: SAIDI ∈ [0, 500] min/yr, SAIFI ∈ [0, 10] int/yr, GDP per capita ∈ [5,000, 60,000] €, DER ratio ∈ [0, 2.0], population ∈ [100, 5,000,000], unemployment ∈ [0, 30]%, energy poverty ∈ [0, 40]%, heatwave exposure ∈ [0, 1], flood/landslide risk class ∈ [1, 4], seismic zone ∈ [1, 4], PGA ∈ [0, 0.5] g, transformer loading ∈ [0, 2.0]. Values outside these bounds are flagged as errors; values in the outer 10% are flagged as warnings.

Layer 2 — Geographic validation: All coordinates are checked against Italy's bounding box: latitude ∈ [35.5°, 47.1°], longitude ∈ [6.6°, 18.6°]. Substations outside these bounds are rejected (likely data entry errors or non-Italian records).

Layer 3 — Territory class validation: ARERA territory class values must be one of {alta_concentrazione, media_concentrazione, bassa_concentrazione}.

Layer 4 — Cross-validation: Provincial aggregates are cross-checked against regional totals. If provincial population sums diverge from the regional total by more than 5%, a warning is raised. This catches data version mismatches between ISTAT provincial and regional datasets.

Layer 5 — Freshness validation: File modification dates are checked against DATA_FRESHNESS_THRESHOLD_DAYS (default: 365). Stale files generate warnings to prompt data refresh.

0.3.12.3   Data Audit Trail — Regulatory Compliance

DataAuditTrail — Provenance Tracking AuditReport = { files: [FileMetadata], imputations: [ImputationRecord], validations: [ValidationEvent], source_mappings: [SourceMapping] }
The DataAuditTrail class in audit_trail.py provides complete data provenance tracking for regulatory and investment-grade audit requirements. Four tracking dimensions:

File metadata: For every ingested file: original filename, absolute path, SHA256 checksum, file size (bytes), modification timestamp, format (CSV/JSON/YAML), and row count. The SHA256 hash enables reproducibility verification — re-running the pipeline on the same input files must produce identical outputs.

Imputation logging: Whenever the DataLoader falls back to hardcoded values (because a real file is unavailable), an ImputationRecord is created: source name, field name, imputed value, reason ("file not found" / "field missing" / "value out of range"), and timestamp. The imputation summary reports the total imputation rate (fraction of fields using defaults vs real data).

Validation events: Every validation pass or failure is logged: source name, validator type (range/geo/territory/cross/freshness), result (pass/fail/warning), and details. This creates a complete validation history that can be presented to auditors.

Source mappings: For substation-level outputs, each SSI score component is traced back to its source file and row number. This enables end-to-end provenance: "The SAIDI value for substation #12345 came from row 847 of arera_tiqe_2024.csv, file hash a3b7c9...".

Export: The audit trail exports to both JSON (machine-readable, for pipeline integration) and XLSX (human-readable, for regulatory submission). The XLSX export produces a multi-sheet workbook with separate sheets for files, imputations, validations, and source mappings.
Connector architecture update: The seven data connectors (ARERA, GSE, ISPRA, ISTAT, INGV, DESI, OSM) in connectors/ have been updated with two new methods: validate_loaded_data() (calls DataValidator on the connector's loaded dataframe) and get_source_mapping() (returns SourceMapping records for provenance tracking). The FleetBuilder orchestrator in connectors/fleet_builder.py now implements error recovery: a single connector failure does not block the remaining connectors. Failed connectors log their error and the pipeline continues with partial data, flagging the gap in the audit trail.
Implementation: Three new modules: data_validator.py, data_loader.py, audit_trail.py. Updated: config.py (VALIDATION_RANGES, ITALY_BOUNDS), connectors/base.py, connectors/fleet_builder.py, real_data.py, schemas.py (new data_quality_report and data_source_manifest fields on Layer1Output). 83 new tests. Committed in ssi-layer1 v1.2.1 (commit 76102c6).

0.3.15   Actuarial Catastrophe Pricing (CAT/EVT/TVaR/PAD) ✓ CODED — v4.2

The actuarial framework integrates catastrophe modelling from the (re)insurance industry. Four layers price black swan risk explicitly: Layer A (CAT Event-Loss Table: 10 archetypes × 3 families), Layer B (EVT: GPD tail replacement, ξ=0.20–0.40), Layer C (TVaR: extracted from existing MC), Layer D (PAD: +50–120 bps on WACC). Phase 1 requires zero new data sources.

0.3.17   Cannibalization Resilience — Locality-Adjusted Revenue Protection ✓ CODED — v4.2 (Session 16→17)

As BESS fleet deployment accelerates under PNIEC (Italy: 7.4 GW at end-2025, targeting 70–80 GW new renewables by 2030), cannibalization risk emerges as a fourth risk family. However, cannibalization is structurally asymmetric: system-level active power services (arbitrage, aFRR, capacity market) are fully exposed, but local reactive power and power quality services are immune — because reactive power is a local phenomenon that cannot be solved by transporting active power from afar.

Key insight: Substations with high SSI scores have revenue stacks dominated by local, non-cannibalizable services. The SSI score itself is a cannibalization resilience indicator.

Revenue StreamLocality λCannibalization Exposure
Arbitrage (R1)0.05HIGH — spreads compress with fleet growth
aFRR/mFRR (R2/R3)0.10HIGH — clearing prices fall
Capacity Market (R4)0.15MEDIUM — national competition
DSO Flexibility (R8)0.85LOW — local constraint-bound
PQaaS / Reactive Power0.95NEAR-ZERO — physics-bound local
Climate Resilience (R11)0.90LOW — location-dependent

8 Cannibalization Enrichments:

IDEnrichmentPhaseFormula
CANN-P1-1Zonal BESS SaturationP1BESS_SAT(z) = BESS_MW(z) / Peak_Load(z)
CANN-P1-2Locality FactorsP1λ_i ∈ [0.05, 0.95] per revenue stream
CANN-P1-3Cannibalization Resilience ScoreP1CRS(s) = Σ(w_i × λ_i) / Σ w_i
CANN-P1-4Revenue Haircut (convex)P1R_adj = R × [1 − (1−λ) × δ × (SAT/x_crit)^γ]
CANN-P2-1Forward Saturation PathP2Stochastic BESS deployment from PNIEC + GSE
CANN-P2-2Merit Order DisplacementP2Spread_adj = Spread × exp(−β × SAT)
CANN-P2-3Cannibalization-PADP2PAD × (1 + κ_cann × (1 − CRS))
CANN-P3-1Stochastic Cannibalization MCP3Per-iteration SAT path × locality-adjusted haircuts
Strategic conclusion: The SSI-ENN’s substation ranking inherently selects for cannibalization resilience. High-SSI MV substations have CRS > 0.70, meaning >70% of their revenue stack is immune to BESS fleet growth. Reactive power demand increases over time (non-linear loads, climate stress) while remaining non-cannibalizable — the opposite of system-level arbitrage. The CRS formalises this structural hedge for investor communication: “Our portfolio CRS is 0.78 — 78% of revenue is immune to fleet growth.”

0.3.18   Nodal Pricing Scenario — Zonal → Nodal Transition Model ✓ CODED — v4.2 (Session 18)

Italy currently operates a 6-zone bidding system (Nord, Centro-Nord, Centro-Sud, Sud, Sicilia, Sardegna). The EU Clean Energy Package and ACER guidance push toward nodal/locational marginal pricing. A zonal→nodal transition would fundamentally restructure cannibalization dynamics, creating congestion-based natural moats for well-sited BESS and shifting revenue from system-level arbitrage (R1, λ=0.05) toward locational premium (R7, λ=0.50+). This section models the transition as a regime-switch scenario overlay on the base zonal framework.

NODAL-1: Node-Level BESS Saturation BESS_SAT_nodal(n) = BESS_MW_node / Load_node Phase 1: BESS_SAT_zonal × (1 − 0.3 × CF) — congestion limits deployment at constrained nodes
NODAL-2: Congestion-Boosted Locality Factor λ_R7_nodal(n) = λ_R7_base + ψ × CF(n) where ψ = 0.30 (congestion boost coefficient), CF(n) = zone congestion factor [0, 1], cap at 0.95
NODAL-3: Nodal CRS Adjustment CRS_nodal(s) = CRS_zonal × (1 + η × CF) + shift_effect where η = 0.15, shift_effect = 0.40 × (λ_R7 − λ_R1) × CF × w_R1 — revenue redistribution from R1→R7
NODAL-4: Option F Integration Value NPV_Δ = V_RO × P(nodal) × E[CRS_uplift] × discount where P(nodal) = 0.06/yr (∼45% within 10 years), discount = 0.92 (risk-adjusted PV factor)
ZoneCongestion FactorCRS Upliftλ_R7 (nodal)Calibration Basis
Nord0.25+6.8%0.575Well-meshed, but N→S bottleneck
Centro-Nord0.35+9.5%0.605Toscana renewables vs Rome demand
Centro-Sud0.45+12.2%0.635Structural N→S congestion
Sud0.55+14.9%0.665Wind curtailment, weak interconnectors
Sicilia0.70+19.0%0.710Island, limited HVDC capacity
Sardegna0.65+17.6%0.695Island, wind surplus
Revenue redistribution mechanism. Under nodal pricing, 40% of R1 (arbitrage, w=0.25) migrates to R7 (LMP premium, w=0.05): R1 weight drops from 0.25→0.15, R7 weight rises from 0.05→0.15. Since λ_R7 >> λ_R1 (0.50+ vs 0.05), this shift structurally improves CRS — from 0.372 to 0.407 fleet median. For congested nodes (Sicilia, Sardegna), the combined uplift from congestion immunity + revenue shift produces CRS_nodal > 0.44 — approaching the “Resilient” threshold. This means the zonal→nodal transition is structurally positive for well-sited BESS — the regulatory change enhances rather than destroys value.
Connection to Option F and BS-F2-1. The NPV_Δ metric integrates directly with the LSMC Option F (Market Evolution: LMP + PQaaS + CRS) via probability-weighted CRS uplift. It also connects to BS-F2-1 (regulatory regime shift, annual P=0.08) — the nodal transition is a specific instance of the regulatory regime shift class. Data source CN.4 (Terna Congestion Data) provides zone-level congestion rents; Phase 2 will use node-level granularity from Terna GRTN.

0.3.19   Fuel-Electricity Nexus — Black Swan Family 4 (BS-F4) ✓ CODED — v3.2

The Fuel-Electricity Nexus (BS-F4) captures geopolitical and commodity shocks that propagate through fuel supply chains to affect electricity prices and thermal generation availability. Italy's thermal generation fleet (40–50% of winter peak load) creates a critical link: when gas or oil prices spike exogenously, thermal plant heat rates increase, pushing electricity prices higher. A well-sited BESS captures this arbitrage opportunity.

BS-F4-1: Geopolitical Fuel-Price Shock Scenarios

Shock probabilities (annual):

  • Gas supply disruption (Gazprom-type): 4%
  • Oil embargo / energy security premium: 2%
  • LNG terminal outage: 3%
  • Combined annual shock probability: 8.7%

Calibration sources: 2022 Russia-Ukraine gas crisis precedent, ACER risk framework, TTF forward curve volatility.

BS-F4-2: Fuel-to-Electricity Transmission Mechanism

BS-F4-2: Fuel Shock → Electricity Price Transmission ΔP_elec(t) = R_conv(t) × ζ_zone × β_pass × (M_fuel − 1) × P_base
R_conv(t) = thermal generation conversion ratio (fuel → electricity)
  2024: 0.58   2030: 0.42   2035: 0.25   2050: 0.05

ζ_zone = zone thermal dependency
  Nord: 0.55   Centro-Nord: 0.50   Centro-Sud: 0.45
  Sud: 0.65   Sicilia: 0.75   Sardegna: 0.70

β_pass = 0.85 (transmission pass-through efficiency)

M_fuel = fuel price multiplier
  Gas: 4.0×   Oil: 2.5×   LNG: 3.0× (max applied)

P_base = baseline electricity price (ENTSO-E P90 reference)

Effect: A 2.5× gas price shock in Sicilia (ζ=0.75) during 2024 (R_conv=0.58) generates a +€74/MWh electricity price spike, creating a high-margin BESS discharge opportunity.

BS-F4-3: BESS Arbitrage Upside Under Fuel Stress

BS-F4-3: Annual BESS Fuel-Nexus Revenue V_fuel = φ × ΔP_elec × MWh_annual × cycles_per_year × η_rt / WACC
φ = 0.40 (spread pass-through factor)
η_rt = 0.87 (roundtrip efficiency, Li-ion)
cycles_per_year = ~250–300 dispatch events

Fleet results (Session 17 deployment):

  • Median fuel-nexus upside: 0.0687 (€68.7k per MW)
  • BESS fuel value: 0.6236 (62.36% of total under stress)
  • Decarbonisation discount: 0.3182 (31.82% haircut by 2050)

BS-F4-4: Decarbonisation Trajectory & Temporal Value Decay

YearR_convThermal Capacity (GW)Renewable %V_fuel (relative)
20240.5820.834%1.000
20300.4218.252%0.685
20350.2515.668%0.385
20500.053–595%+0.035

Strategic implication: F4 upside is largest in the 2024–2030 window. BESS siting should prioritise high-ζ_zone locations (Sud, Sicilia, Sardegna) to capture fuel-nexus value while thermal capacity remains material. Post-2035, electricity pricing is dominated by renewable availability and grid services (reactive power), not fuel costs.

BS-F4 Data Sources (BS-DS.1 through BS-DS.4)

Source IDNameDataPhaseAccess
BS-DS.1GME MGPDay-ahead zonal prices, high-price hour frequencyP1REST API (existing)

Connection to Actuarial Framework. BS-F4 shocks are integrated as two new event archetypes in the CAT event-loss table: FUEL_GAS_SPIKE (freq 0.04/yr) and FUEL_OIL_SHOCK (freq 0.02/yr). Unlike Family 1 events (weather) which harm BESS through forced outages, BS-F4 events improve BESS value through price spikes. The PAD framework is extended: PAD_fuel = PAD_base × (1 + κ_fuel × (1 − R_conv(t))), reflecting increasing valuation uncertainty as thermal capacity declines and fuel-shock pass-through becomes less reliable.

0.3.20   Monte Carlo Revenue Engine ✓ CODED — v3.3

The Monte Carlo revenue engine replaces the ad-hoc valuation formulas in bess_valuator.py with a proper stochastic simulation. It generates hourly electricity prices via an Ornstein-Uhlenbeck jump-diffusion process calibrated against published Italian market data (GME, Terna, ARERA), then simulates 10 distinct BESS revenue streams over 25 years, across 200 independent paths per substation.

Price Model

The electricity price follows a mean-reverting jump-diffusion process:

dP = κ(μ(t,h) − P)dt + σzone·P·dW + J·dN(λ)

where μ(t,h) = Pbase,zone × seasonal(month) × intraday(hour) × weekend(dow), κ = 50/yr (half-life ~5 days), σ = 0.35 annual × zone volatility multiplier, J ~ LogNormal(0.50, 0.40) for positive spikes, N(λ) = Poisson(12/yr).

Calibration from Published Italian Market Data

ARERACapacity Market€33k/MW/year

ARERAmFRR activation rate~15% of hours

0.3.21   NN v3.3 — MC-Derived Training Targets ✓ CODED — v3.3

In v3.2 and earlier, the Neural Network (Layer 3) trained on targets derived from the ad-hoc formulas in bess_valuator.py, creating a circular dependency between Layer 2 and Layer 3. In v3.3, the NPV Regressor's target variable is switched from the formula-derived NPV_M to the Monte Carlo-derived npv_P50 (median of 200 stochastic paths). This breaks the circular dependency and grounds the NN in genuine economic simulation.
