Section 0.4

Layer 2 — Valuation Architecture ✓ Implemented v0.1.0–v0.3.0

Layer 2 transforms the SSI v4.0 data (Layer 1) into investment-grade financial outputs. It comprises three interconnected engines: the BESS Value-Add Engine (Phase 1: DNO and Community value stacks), the Cost and Revenue Engine (Phase 2: TCO, revenue stack, dispatch optimisation), and the Real Options Pricing Engine (Phase 2: six embedded options valued via lattice and LSMC methods). This section provides the complete mathematical specification for every formula in the valuation pipeline — from SSI sub-metric inputs to final option-adjusted NPV outputs.

The Layer 2 formulae share a critical architectural property: every equation is config-parameterised. Each formula is evaluated twice — once for Config A (4.9 MW / 19.6 MWh) and once for Config B (9.9 MW / 39.6 MWh) — producing parallel valuation tracks that are compared in the final output. Subscript c ∈ {A, B} throughout denotes the configuration. Where behaviour differs between configs, this is noted explicitly.

0.4.1   DNO Value Stack Formulae (D1–D5)

The DNO value stack quantifies avoided or deferred costs from the perspective of the distribution network operator. All five components are computed per substation j and per configuration c.

D1 — Investment Deferral D1_j,c(t) = C_reinf,j × [1/(1+WACC)^t_0 − 1/(1+WACC)^(t_0 + Δt_c)] × (1+WACC)^t
Concept: A BESS at substation j defers conventional reinforcement (transformer upgrade, line rebuild, new substation) by Δt years. D1 is the NPV benefit of postponing C_reinf from its original trigger year t_0 to the deferred year t_0 + Δt.

C_reinf,j = estimated reinforcement cost at substation j (€). Derived from:
 • Asset condition: I5 (age) and C1/C2 (reliability deficit indicating imminent replacement need)
 • Network density: I4 (substations/km²) — lower density → longer feeders → higher reinforcement unit cost
 • Topology: I6 (meshing ratio) — radial networks have fewer reinforcement options, higher per-scheme cost
 • Territory class: ARERA publishes average reinforcement unit costs by territory class (alta/media/bassa concentrazione). Range: €0.3–2.5M per substation scheme depending on scope.

t_0 = baseline reinforcement trigger year without BESS. Estimated from: T1 (DER trajectory — when S1 Dimovski crosses the hosting-capacity threshold) and I5 (asset age — when assets reach end-of-life). t_0 is the earlier of (a) DER-driven capacity trigger and (b) age-driven replacement trigger.

Δt_c = deferral duration provided by BESS config c. The BESS defers reinforcement by absorbing the load growth / DER surplus that would otherwise trigger the investment. Δt_c = f(P_BESS,c / ΔP_growth,j) where ΔP_growth is the annual load/DER growth rate at substation j. Config B (9.9 MW) typically provides 1.5–2.5× the deferral duration of Config A (4.9 MW), but the relationship is sub-linear because the reinforcement trigger is not purely capacity-driven — some triggers (fault level, protection coordination) are not addressable by BESS alone.

WACC = regulated weighted average cost of capital for the relevant DNO (Italy: ARERA determination ~5.5% real pre-tax for 2022–2027 regulatory period; UK: Ofgem RIIO-2 ~3.2%; DE: BNetzA ~5.1%). Country-specific parameter.

Annualised form: D1_annual,j,c = D1_j,c(NPV) / Σ_{t=1}^{30} 1/(1+r)^t. This converts the lump-sum deferral benefit into an equivalent annual value for comparability with other stack components.
D2 — Congestion Relief (Avoided Curtailment) D2_j,c(t) = E_curtailed,j(t) × min(1, E_BESS,c / E_curtailed,j(t)) × (P_wholesale(t) + P_subsidy(t))
E_curtailed,j(t) = estimated annual curtailed DER energy at substation j in year t (MWh/year). Derived from:
 • S1_j (Dimovski ratio): for S1 > 0.7, curtailment begins; for S1 > 1.0, curtailment is routine
 • S2_j (RPF hours): each RPF hour with generation exceeding transformer capacity produces curtailed energy = (P_gen − P_transformer) × 1hr
 • T1_j(τ): curtailment grows over time as DER increases. E_curtailed,j(t) = E_curtailed,j(0) × [S1_j(t)/S1_j(0)]^β where β ≈ 2.0–3.0 captures the nonlinear ramp above hosting capacity

E_BESS,c = daily BESS energy throughput available for curtailment absorption = P_rated,c × duration × η(t) × 365 (MWh/year). Config A: ≤ 7,154 MWh/year; Config B: ≤ 14,454 MWh/year at BoL. The min(1, ...) term caps BESS absorption at the total curtailed volume — the BESS cannot absorb more than is curtailed.

P_wholesale(t) = wholesale electricity price in year t (€/MWh). Stochastic: modelled as mean-reverting process with regime-switching (Markov) to capture structural shifts (energy transition, carbon pricing). Historical calibration from GME (Italy) or equivalent power exchange.

P_subsidy(t) = renewable support premium forgone due to curtailment (€/MWh). Applicable where DER operator loses incentive payments for curtailed generation. Italy: Conto Energia FiT (legacy installations) or FER1 auctions (newer). Declining over time as older incentive schemes expire.
D3 — Loss Reduction D3_j,c(t) = E_throughput,j(t) × LF_j × [1 − (1 − ΔI/I_peak,j)²] × P_wholesale(t) × MLF_j
Concept: Losses on distribution feeders are proportional to I² (current squared). Peak-shaving by the BESS reduces peak current by ΔI, yielding quadratic loss savings.

E_throughput,j(t) = annual energy throughput at substation j (MWh). From SSI load-profile estimation (C-component patterns + E-component sectoral mix).

LF_j = feeder loss factor at substation j (%). Estimated from I6 (topology — longer radial feeders have higher losses), I4 (density — sparse networks = longer average feeder length), and published DNO loss data. Italian distribution losses average 4.5–6.5% depending on region; substation-level LF_j ranges from ~2% (dense urban, short feeders) to ~10% (rural, long radial feeders).

ΔI/I_peak,j = fractional peak current reduction from BESS peak-shaving. ΔI = P_BESS,c / (√3 × V_MV × cos φ). For Config A (4.9 MW): typical ΔI/I_peak ≈ 8–25% depending on transformer rating. Config B (9.9 MW): ≈ 15–45%.

MLF_j = marginal loss factor at substation j. Published by TSOs for transmission nodes; estimated at distribution level from network models. MLF > 1 at end-of-feeder substations (losses are above average); MLF < 1 near primary substations.

Quadratic term: [1 − (1 − ΔI/I_peak)²] is the fractional reduction in I²R losses from reducing peak current by ΔI. At ΔI/I_peak = 20%, loss reduction = 36%. The quadratic relationship means larger BESS (higher ΔI) provides disproportionate loss savings — Config B's D3 is typically 2.5–4× Config A's, not merely 2×.
D4 — Voltage Support D4_j,c(t) = N_violations,j(t) × (1 − ρ_BESS,c) × C_penalty,j + Q_BESS,c × P_reactive
Two components:

Component 1 — Avoided EN 50160 violations:
N_violations,j(t) = number of EN 50160 voltage exceedances per year at substation j (from SSI V1_j).
ρ_BESS,c = fraction of violations mitigated by BESS reactive-power injection and voltage support. ρ depends on: (a) BESS inverter rating (Config A: 4.9 MVA; Config B: 9.9 MVA — the full inverter capacity is available for reactive power even when the battery is empty, since STATCOM mode requires no stored energy), (b) electrical distance from BESS to point of violation, (c) whether violation is caused by active-power imbalance (BESS can address directly) or harmonic distortion (requires active filtering capability). Typical ρ_BESS: 0.4–0.8 for Config A; 0.6–0.95 for Config B.
C_penalty,j = cost per violation. In quality-regulated systems (UK under Ofgem IIS, Italy under ARERA TIQE), DNOs face explicit penalties for exceeding voltage-quality thresholds. In non-penalty systems, C_penalty is estimated from CEER willingness-to-pay surveys for improved PQ. Range: €500–5,000 per violation event depending on severity, customer count affected, and regulatory regime.

Component 2 — Reactive power service revenue:
Q_BESS,c = reactive power capability of the BESS inverter (MVAr). Equal to full inverter rating when operating in STATCOM mode with no active-power dispatch; reduced during charge/discharge by Q = √(S² − P²).
P_reactive = price of reactive power (€/MVAr-h). Currently €0 in most European markets (reactive power is mandated but uncompensated). This term is modelled as an emerging revenue — probability-weighted within Option F (PQaaS component). UK TNUoS reactive power charges provide a reference: ~£2–5/MVAr-h at constrained nodes.
D5 — Fault Level Contribution D5_j,c = 𝟙[I4_j < θ_density AND I6_j < θ_mesh] × FLAG
D5 is a binary qualitative flag, not monetised in the base case. A substation is flagged as benefiting from BESS fault-level contribution when both I4 (density) and I6 (meshing) fall below their respective thresholds — indicating a weak feeder where additional fault current from the BESS inverter would improve protection coordination.

θ_density ≈ 0.3 substations/km² (below this, feeders are long and fault levels are low).
θ_mesh ≈ 0.15 (below this, the feeder is essentially radial with no alternative fault-current path).

Modern grid-forming BESS inverters can provide programmable fault current (up to 1.5× rated current for 150–200ms), improving protection relay coordination on weak feeders. This is increasingly required by grid codes (UK ENA EREC G99 Issue 6; Germany VDE-AR-N 4110) but not yet monetised in any European market.

D5 is reported as: (a) binary flag (Y/N) for each substation, (b) estimated fault-current contribution (kA) from each BESS config, (c) qualitative assessment of protection coordination improvement. Not included in V_DNO NPV sum but listed on the Value-Add Card as additional DNO benefit — relevant for DSO flexibility procurement (R5) negotiations.
DNO Value-Add — Aggregated V_DNO,j,c = Σ_{t=1}^{30} [D1_j,c(t) + D2_j,c(t) + D3_j,c(t) + D4_j,c(t)] / (1 + WACC)^t
30-year NPV at country-specific regulated WACC. D5 excluded (qualitative flag). Each D-component is year-dependent: D1 steps down after the original reinforcement would have occurred; D2 grows with DER penetration trajectory (T1); D3 is approximately stable; D4 declines if DNO invests in separate voltage regulation (or increases if PQ degrades with DER growth).

Config comparison: V_DNO,j,B / V_DNO,j,A is typically 1.4–2.2× (not 2.0× — sub-linear scaling on D1 and D4, super-linear on D3 due to quadratic loss effect). The ratio varies by substation SSI profile: at high-S substations (DER-stressed), D2 dominates and scaling is nearly linear; at high-C substations (reliability-poor), D1 dominates and scaling is sub-linear.

0.4.1a   ESG Value Uplift Framework (E1–E3) NEW

VESG quantifies the socio-economic externality value that the BESS generates beyond the regulated DNO avoided-cost framework (D1–D5). It is not a revenue stream to the investor, nor is it added to VDNO. It sits alongside VDNO and VCommunity in the Three-Value Framework as a supplementary metric for: (i) EU Taxonomy alignment reporting (Regulation 2020/852); (ii) SFDR PAI indicator disclosure (Regulation 2019/2088, Indicators 1, 3, 6); (iii) ESG-linked financing term negotiation (green bond eligibility, WACC reduction); and (iv) policy dialogue with ARERA, MiSE, and regional authorities.

Scope boundary: VESG is computed per substation j and per configuration c, using the same Monte Carlo framework (10,000 iterations) as D1–D5. It is reported as a separate line in the Value-Add Card with a confidence interval (P10/P50/P90), never aggregated into VDNO. A cross-reference matrix ensures no double-counting with D2 (congestion), W2 (carbon displacement), or W3 (employment).
VESG — Composite Formula V_ESG,j,c = E1_carbon,j,c + E2_greenium,j,c + E3_social,j,c
Each component is a 30-year NPV. E1 and E3 are discounted at the project discount rate (WACC for investor perspective) or social discount rate (3.0% for societal perspective). E2 is inherently a financing-cost differential and does not require separate discounting. All three components are independently auditable against published regulatory or market-practice data sources (see Data Source Table below).
E1 — Avoided Carbon Externality E1_j,c = Σ_{t=1}^{30} [ Q_disp,j,c(t) × EF_marginal(t) × P_carbon(t) ] / (1 + r)^t
Concept: Shadow value of CO&sub2; emissions displaced when the BESS shifts load from fossil marginal plant to stored renewable energy. Monetised at a shadow carbon price.

Q_disp,j,c(t) = displaced fossil generation in year t (MWh). Derived from SSI-ENN dispatch model: BESS capacity (MW) × equivalent full cycles/year × duration (h) × round-trip efficiency × (1 − curtailment factor). Only counts net incremental displaced MWh beyond what D2 already captures — i.e., arbitrage + frequency response dispatch volumes, excluding congestion-specific dispatch (which is D2’s domain). The dispatch model tags each MWh with its primary service to prevent double-counting.

EF_marginal(t) = marginal emission factor (tCO&sub2;/MWh) of the plant the BESS displaces. Country- and zone-specific. Primary source: ISPRA annual “Efficiency and Decarbonization Indicators” report (R-404/2024 series). Cross-check: JRC GHG Emission Factors for Electricity Consumption (EU dataset). For Italy (Southern/Sicily zone), the marginal plant is predominantly CCGT gas: EF ≈ 0.37–0.42 tCO&sub2;/MWh. EF_marginal is modelled as declining over time (−0.5–1.0%/yr) as the grid decarbonises, consistent with PNIEC 2030 targets.

P_carbon(t) = shadow carbon price (€/tCO&sub2;) in year t. Three scenarios are mandatory:
 • Floor: EU ETS spot price (ICE Endex EUA settlement). 2025 reference: ~€75/tCO&sub2;. Forward curve from Enerdata/BNEF.
 • Central: Mid-point between ETS and SCC ≈ €125/tCO&sub2;. Real escalation +2%/yr.
 • Ceiling: US EPA Social Cost of Carbon (2023 update, 2% discount rate: ~$190/tCO&sub2; ≈ €175). Converted at ECB reference rate.

r = discount rate. WACC_regulated for DNO perspective; WACC_equity for investor perspective; 3.0% for societal perspective.

Illustrative E1 (central, Config B): 15,000 MWh/yr × 0.38 tCO&sub2;/MWh × €125/tCO&sub2; = €712,500/yr. 30-year NPV at 5.2% ≈ €10.6M. Range: €6.4M (ETS floor) – €14.8M (SCC ceiling). E1 is the dominant component (~80% of VESG).
E2 — Green Financing Premium (Greenium) E2_j,c = Σ_{t=1}^{30} [ CAPEX_outstanding,c(t) × ΔWACC_green ] / (1 + r)^t
Concept: NPV of the blended WACC reduction attributable to EU Taxonomy alignment. The BESS qualifies under Climate Delegated Act, Annex I, Activity 4.10 (“electricity storage”), enabling access to green bond markets and ESG-screened equity — both of which compress cost of capital.

ΔWACC_green = blended WACC reduction (bps). Composed of:
 • Debt greenium: 3–15 bps on senior secured infrastructure debt (source: Amundi/Climate Bonds Initiative H2 2024; BNEF green bond pricing)
 • Equity CoE reduction: 10–30 bps (source: MSCI ESG Ratings & Cost of Capital empirical study 2023; ScienceDirect cross-sectional evidence 2025)
 • Blended at project D/E ratio (typically 70:30): central estimate 15–25 bps

CAPEX_outstanding,c(t) = outstanding capital subject to financing cost in year t. Follows the project finance amortisation schedule (typically 18-year debt tenor, straight-line amortisation).

Illustrative E2 (Config B): Avg outstanding capital ~€4.0M × 20 bps = €8,000/yr. 30-year NPV ≈ €0.12M. E2 is the smallest component — its primary value is structural (Taxonomy alignment signal) rather than absolute NPV.
E3 — Social & Community Co-benefit E3_j,c = Σ_{t=1}^{30} [ AQ_j,c(t) + VOLL_j,c(t) + JOB_j,c(t) ] / (1 + r)^t
Three sub-components:

AQ_j,c(t) — Avoided air pollution damage: Q_disp,j,c(t) × pollutant EF (NOx, SO&sub2;, PM2.5 per MWh of fossil displaced, from EEA inventory) × damage cost per tonne (ExternE/NEEDS methodology, updated by EEA “Air Quality in Europe” 2023). Reference damage costs: NOx ~€15,000/t, PM2.5 ~€40,000/t, SO&sub2; ~€12,000/t. Range: €45,000–90,000/yr for Config B.

VOLL_j,c(t) — Avoided Value of Lost Load: Expected unserved energy during BESS-mitigatable events (MWh) × VOLL (€/MWh). ACER/CEER “Methodology for Calculating VoLL” provides Italy-specific estimates: €10,000–15,000/MWh (residential + commercial blend). Only counts incremental resilience beyond what W1 (SAIDI improvement) already captures — i.e., sub-hourly frequency events and partial outages not reflected in SAIDI statistics. Range: €20,000–50,000/yr.

JOB_j,c(t) — Local employment & skills transfer: Construction-phase FTE × local wage premium (2 years), plus O&M-phase FTE × local multiplier (28 years). Source: IRENA “Renewable Energy and Jobs” Annual Review; ISTAT local labour statistics. Only counts incremental FTE beyond what W3 (community employment) already captures — i.e., the skills-transfer premium for battery-storage specialisation, training programme value, and supply-chain localisation effect. Range: €30,000–60,000/yr (avg, front-loaded).

Double-counting protocol: E3 measures residual social co-benefits after deducting W2 (carbon displacement — handled by E1 instead, with the ETS/SCC price difference representing the additional externality layer) and W3 (base employment value). The cross-reference matrix is:
 • E1 vs W2: W2 has been restructured (v27a) to report physical tCO&sub2;/yr only. All monetary carbon valuation is consolidated in E1. No overlap exists — E1 owns the € layer; W2 owns the tCO&sub2; layer. Both use the same underlying Q_disp × MEF calculation.
 • E3.JOB vs W3: W3 counts direct FTE and land lease. E3.JOB counts the skills-premium and supply-chain localisation multiplier effect only.
 • E3.VOLL vs W1: W1 uses annual average SAIDI reduction. E3.VOLL uses sub-hourly frequency-event distribution tail.

Illustrative E3 (central, Config B): (€67k + €35k + €45k)/yr = €147,000/yr. 30-year NPV at 5.2% ≈ €2.2M. Range: €1.4M – €3.0M.

VESG Illustrative Range (Config B, per substation)

Validation Framework

GateTestApplied ToMethod
V1Regulatory GroundingE1, E2, E3Each variable must trace to a published regulation, delegated act, or recognised methodology (EU Taxonomy, EU ETS, ISPRA, ACER, EEA ExternE). No purely proprietary inputs.
V2AdditionalityE1 primarilyDisplaced emissions must be additional — the BESS operation causes fossil displacement that would not occur without it. Validated by comparing dispatch-model counterfactual (no-BESS scenario) to actual dispatch.
V3No Double-CountingE1 vs D2; E3 vs W1–W3Cross-reference matrix (above) ensures E1 uses only net incremental displaced MWh beyond D2, and E3 sub-components use only residual values beyond W1–W3. Dispatch model tags each MWh with primary service.
V4Sensitivity & BoundsAllMonte Carlo (10,000 iterations) varying P_carbon (±40%), EF_marginal (±15%), Q_disp (±20%), VOLL (±50%). Report P10/P50/P90.

Governance Requirements

Data Vintage Rule: All inputs must use the most recent available vintage at time of calculation. No input may be older than T−3 years from report date. Carbon Price Lock: P_carbon must be locked to a specific observation date (disclosed in report). Scenario Disclosure: All three P_carbon scenarios (floor/central/ceiling) must be reported; the central estimate uses the mid-point. Additionality Statement: Each report must include a statement that avoided emissions are additional and not claimed elsewhere (not sold as carbon credits or Guarantees of Origin). Independent Verification: VESG is subject to limited assurance (ISAE 3000) aligned with SFDR PAI reporting. Refresh Cycle: VESG must be recalculated annually within 90 days of ISPRA publication.

0.4.2   Community Value Stack Formulae (W1–W5)

The community value stack quantifies social and economic value to the local community. Discounted at the social discount rate (3.0% real, per EU Commission CBA guidance) — lower than the DNO WACC, reflecting the broader societal perspective.

W1 — Reliability Improvement W1_j,c(t) = ΔSAIDI_j,c × E2_j × N_customers,j × (1 − δ_overlap,j)
ΔSAIDI_j,c = reduction in SAIDI at substation j from BESS backup/islanding (minutes/customer/year). The BESS can supply the local feeder during upstream outages for up to its rated duration: Config A: 4 hours (240 minutes); Config B: 4 hours (240 minutes — same duration, higher power). ΔSAIDI = min(C1_j, 240) × P(outage occurs during BESS availability window). For typical Italian substations, ΔSAIDI ≈ 15–120 minutes/customer/year — bounded below by low-C substations (few outages to mitigate) and above by the BESS duration limit.

E2_j = customer-mix-weighted VoLL at substation j (€/kWh unserved). From Layer 1 §0.3.2. Converted to €/minute via: E2_minute = E2_j × P_avg,j / (60 × N_customers,j) where P_avg,j is average demand at the substation.

N_customers,j = total customers served by the substation. Italian range: ~200 (small rural) to ~15,000 (urban).

δ_overlap,j = overlap correction with D1. The reliability improvement captured in W1 (social VoLL perspective) and D1 (DNO cost perspective) both derive from the same physical phenomenon — reduced outage time. δ_overlap prevents double-counting by subtracting the fraction of W1 benefit already captured as avoided-penalty-cost in the DNO stack. Typically δ ≈ 0.15–0.30 depending on whether the DNO faces explicit reliability incentives (UK IIS: high overlap) or not (most continental European DNOs: low overlap, since DNO penalties don't fully reflect social VoLL).

Config A vs B: W1 is identical for both configs at the same substation because both provide the same 4-hour duration. The power difference (4.9 vs 9.9 MW) affects how many feeders can be simultaneously backed up, but within a single substation's service area, both configs can island the local feeder.
W2 — Carbon Displacement (Physical Metric + Monetisation Reference) REVISED W2_j,c(t) = E_displaced,j,c(t) × MEF_country(t)  [tCO₂/year — physical metric]
Restructured in v27a: W2 is now a physical-metric component, reporting avoided emissions in tCO₂/year. The monetary valuation of displaced carbon has been consolidated into E1 (§0.4.1a) to eliminate double-counting between the Community stack and the ESG stack. W2 retains its critical role in community-facing reporting: tCO₂/year is the metric required for EU Taxonomy substantial-contribution evidence, SFDR PAI Indicator 1, PNIEC progress reporting, and local government climate-action plans. The physical metric is independent of carbon price assumptions, making it more robust for policy dialogue.

E_displaced,j,c(t) = total MWh of marginal fossil generation displaced in year t across all BESS dispatch modes — curtailment absorption (from D2), arbitrage, and frequency response. This is the same Q_disp used in E1 (§0.4.1a). The dispatch model tags each MWh with its primary service, ensuring traceability.

MEF_country(t) = marginal emission factor of the national grid (tCO₂/MWh). The marginal generator displaced by BESS dispatch. Italy 2026: ≈ 0.35–0.45 tCO₂/MWh (gas CCGT at the margin). MEF declines over time as the grid decarbonises — modelled as declining trajectory calibrated to PNIEC 2030 targets and NECP pathways. Stochastic in Monte Carlo. Primary source: ISPRA annual report; cross-check: JRC, IEA.

Monetary cross-reference: The €-value of W2 emissions is computed in E1 (§0.4.1a) using the three-scenario shadow carbon price framework (ETS floor / mid-point / SCC ceiling). To obtain a monetary W2 for backwards compatibility with earlier report versions or for community-perspective reporting at the social discount rate:
 W2_monetary,j,c(t) = W2_j,c(t) × SCC(t)
where SCC(t) is the EU Commission recommended social cost of carbon (~€100–150/tCO₂, rising at ~2% real/year). This is reported as a memo item only — not added to V_Community to avoid double-counting with E1.

Additionally reported: tCO₂/year displaced (primary metric), cumulative tCO₂ over 30 years, annual W2 trajectory (declining as MEF declines with grid decarbonisation). Config B displaces approximately 2× Config A (linear scaling with energy capacity).

No double-counting protocol: W2 (physical tCO₂) and E1 (monetary €) use the same underlying Q_disp × MEF calculation. The split ensures: (i) community reporting gets the auditable physical metric (tCO₂); (ii) ESG valuation gets the monetised externality (€, with scenario disclosure); (iii) no MWh is valued twice. The V_Community NPV sum (W1 + W2_physical + W3 + W4 + W5) now excludes the monetary carbon layer — that layer is exclusively in V_ESG via E1.
W3 — Local Economic Impact W3_j,c = [FTE_construction,c × W_local,j × T_construction] + Σ_{t=1}^{30} [FTE_O&M,c × W_local,j + R_land,c + R_tax,c,j] / (1+r)^t
FTE_construction,c = construction-phase full-time equivalent jobs per MW (IRENA BESS employment factors: ~5–8 FTE-years/MW for utility-scale BESS including civil works). Config A: ~25–39 FTE-years; Config B: ~50–79 FTE-years.

FTE_O&M,c = permanent operations and maintenance jobs. ~0.3–0.5 FTE/MW. Config A: ~1.5–2.5 FTE; Config B: ~3.0–5.0 FTE.

W_local,j = average annual wage in the substation's NUTS-3 region (€/year). From Eurostat/ISTAT. Modulated by E1 (GDP per capita) — higher-GDP regions have higher wages but also higher opportunity cost of employment. The net local economic benefit is largest in lower-E1 regions where alternative employment is scarce.

R_land,c = annual land lease payment to landowner (€/year). ~€3,000–8,000/year for Config A footprint (~0.3–0.5 ha); ~€5,000–15,000/year for Config B (~0.5–1.0 ha). Country and location specific.

R_tax,c,j = local tax revenue (IMU/property tax to municipality, business rates). Function of assessed value of the BESS installation and local tax rates.

Reported as both: NPV (€, at social discount rate) and physical metrics (FTE-years construction, FTE permanent, € land lease/year, € local tax/year). The FTE metrics are critical for policy engagement and EU funding applications — BESS deployment as local job creation.
W4 — Resilience Insurance Value W4_j,c(t) = Σ_{h∈{flood,landslide,heat}} P_h,j(t) × L_h,j(t) × M_h,c × [1 + γ_T2,j × (t/10)]
Concept: W4 is the expected avoided economic loss from BESS backup during extreme weather events. It is an actuarial insurance value — the annual premium equivalent of the protection the BESS provides.

P_h,j(t) = annual probability of hazard h affecting substation j in year t. Derived from IRI scores: P_flood = f(I1_j), P_landslide = f(I2_j), P_heat = f(I3_j). These are not static: under CMIP6 SSP2-4.5, P_h increases over the 30-year life (captured by the escalation term below).

L_h,j(t) = expected economic loss given hazard h occurs (€). L = E2_j × N_customers,j × D_outage,h × P_avg,j where D_outage,h is the expected outage duration for hazard type h. D_outage drawn from the tail of the C-component outage distribution (extreme events produce longer outages than the mean SAIDI — conditioning on extreme weather shifts the distribution rightward). Typical D_outage: flood 6–48 hours, landslide 12–72 hours, heat-wave 2–24 hours (transformer derating/failure).

M_h,c = BESS mitigation factor for hazard h under config c. M = min(1, Duration_BESS / D_outage,h). For Config A (4hr): M_flood ≈ 0.2–0.6, M_heat ≈ 0.4–1.0. For Config B (4hr, higher power but same duration): similar M values — duration is the binding constraint, not power. M represents the fraction of the outage duration the BESS can bridge.

Climate escalation: [1 + γ_T2,j × (t/10)] scales W4 upward over the project life. γ_T2,j is the decadal hazard escalation rate from T2: how much worse each hazard gets per decade under SSP2-4.5. For I3 (heat-wave) in southern Italy: γ ≈ 0.15–0.30 (15–30% more frequent per decade). For I1 (flood) in northern Italy: γ ≈ 0.08–0.15. This escalation makes W4 increasingly material in out-years — a finding invisible to static insurance valuations.

Methodological note: W4 is valued using real options methodology — the insurance value is an option (the BESS provides protection if an extreme event occurs), not a certain cashflow. The probability-weighted expected value is the risk-neutral option price. For risk-averse communities (most), the certainty-equivalent value exceeds the expected value — a social welfare correction applied when computing the policy-relevant community value.
W5 — Catastrophic Communications Resilience W5_j,c(t) = P_extended,j(t) × [C_emergency,j + C_economic,j + C_trust,j] × M_comms,c × [1 + γ_T2,j × (t/10)]
W5 is methodologically distinct from W1 and W4. W1 captures average reliability (mean SAIDI × VoLL). W4 captures insurance during extreme weather (electricity layer). W5 captures the communications-cascade cost — the additional harm from losing fibre, mobile, internet, SCADA, and emergency coordination during extended grid failure, beyond and above the electricity loss itself. These are additive: different layers of the same multi-layer cascade.

P_extended,j(t) = probability of extended upstream grid failure (>4 hours) at substation j in year t. Estimated from:
 • Tail of C-component outage distribution (P(SAIDI_event > 240 min) per year, conditioned on extreme events)
 • I-component IRI scores (climate hazards that cause extended outages)
 • T2 climate trajectory (escalation of extreme-event frequency under CMIP6 SSP2-4.5)
 • Historical calibration: multi-day outage records from ARERA / CEER
Critically, P_extended is conditioned on the tail of the outage distribution — communications collapse occurs only during extended events that exhaust telecom backup batteries (2–8 hours). Mean SAIDI is irrelevant; only the right tail matters.

C_emergency,j = emergency-coordination failure cost (€/event). Delayed emergency response × statistical value of life × excess mortality/morbidity from coordination collapse. Calibrated from: Hurricane Maria post-event analysis (Harvard study: ~4,645 excess deaths attributable to infrastructure collapse), Texas 2021 (ERCOT post-mortem: $130B+ total impact), EU Civil Protection case studies. For Italian substations: estimated €0.5–15M per extended-blackout event depending on population served, hospital/care-home density, and emergency-services node coverage. Highly site-specific — driven by E2 (population × VoLL), NIS2 entity register, and CP node density within service area.

C_economic,j = economic cascade cost (€/event). Payment-system downtime (POS terminals, ATMs, online banking), supply-chain coordination loss (just-in-time manufacturing disruption, cold-chain failure), SCADA loss (water/sewage pumping, traffic management, industrial process control). Estimated from E1 (GDP density of service area) × outage duration × economic-activity-disruption factor. For Italian substations: €0.1–5M per event depending on commercial/industrial density.

C_trust,j = institutional trust cost (€/event). Loss of government communication capability during crisis — political economy externality with long-term fiscal consequences. Estimated qualitatively; monetised at 5–15% of C_emergency + C_economic as a scaling factor based on post-event trust surveys (Eurobarometer post-disaster data).

M_comms,c = BESS mitigation factor for communications cascade under config c. This is the fraction of the communications-cascade cost that the BESS can avert. M_comms depends critically on the operating mode:
 (a) Full feeder islanding: M ≈ 0.7–0.95 (sustains entire local area for 4 hours — the BESS duration)
 (b) Selective critical-load supply: M ≈ 0.4–0.8 (sustains base stations, OLTs, emergency nodes for 12–48+ hours)
 Base station load: ~2–5 kW each. OLT load: ~0.5–1 kW each. Emergency node: ~5–20 kW.
 Config A: can sustain ~50–200 base stations for 24+ hours; Config B: ~100–400 base stations for 24+ hours.
 M_comms rises nonlinearly with BESS duration relative to telecom backup-battery exhaustion time (~4–8 hours).

Climate escalation: [1 + γ_T2,j × (t/10)] — same escalation as W4. W5 is the only community value component that grows substantially over the 30-year project life. In southern European substations (high I3, steep T2), W5 can exceed W1 by Year 15–20.

Additionally reported: avoided communications-blackout-hours/year, population protected (count), NIS2 essential entities within service area (count), Civil Protection resilience uplift (qualitative score).
Community Value-Add — Aggregated V_Community,j,c = Σ_{t=1}^{30} [W1_j,c(t) + W2_j,c(t) + W3_j,c(t) + W4_j,c(t) + W5_j,c(t)] / (1 + r_social)^t
r_social = 3.0% real (EU Commission CBA guidance for social infrastructure assessment). Lower than WACC — reflects that society values future community benefits more highly than private investors value future cashflows. This rate differential means V_Community NPV is systematically higher relative to annualised flows than V_DNO NPV.

Time dynamics: W1 and W3 are approximately stable over 30 years. W2 declines slowly as the grid decarbonises (MEF falls). W4 grows with climate escalation (CMIP6 trajectory). W5 grows faster than W4 because it captures the multi-layer cascade that climate events trigger. In the early years, W1 typically dominates; by Year 15–20 in high-I southern European substations, W5 can become the largest component. This time-varying composition is invisible to static community value assessments.

0.4.3   Total Cost of Ownership Model

The TCO model provides the cost side of the NPV calculation. All costs are stochastic — the Monte Carlo engine propagates uncertainty from SSI inputs through cost parameters.

TCO — Lifetime Cost TCO_j,c = CAPEX_c + Σ_{t=1}^{30} [OPEX_c(t) + DEG_j,c(t)] / (1+r)^t + REPOWER_c(t_R1) / (1+r)^{t_R1} + REPOWER_c(t_R2) / (1+r)^{t_R2} + DECOM_c / (1+r)^{30}
CAPEX_c = initial capital expenditure:
 CAPEX = BATT_c + EPC_c + GRID_j,c + ISLAND_c
 BATT_c = battery module cost. Config A: P_rated × duration × cost_kWh = 4.9 MW × 4hr × €350–450/kWh = €6.9–8.8M. Config B: €13.9–17.8M. Stochastic: cost_kWh drawn from BNEF learning-curve distribution.
 EPC_c = engineering, procurement, construction + balance of system. 18–25% of BATT.
 GRID_j,c = grid connection cost. Deterministic per substation (from SSI topology data). Country-specific: Italy €80–300k depending on config and substation location.
 ISLAND_c = islanding and critical-load switchgear. €25–80k depending on config. Unlocks W5 and R11.

OPEX_c(t) = annual operating expenditure:
 OPEX = Fixed_O&M_c + Variable_O&M_c(t) + Insurance + Land_lease
 Fixed_O&M = €8–12/kW/year × P_rated (escalating at 2% real/year for labour cost inflation)
 Variable_O&M = €1.5–2.5/MWh × E_dispatched(t)

DEG_j,c(t) = degradation cost — the implicit economic cost of capacity fade reducing available energy and revenue:
 DEG = Revenue_lost(t) = Rev(config, t | full capacity) − Rev(config, t | degraded capacity)
 Capacity at year t: Cap(t) = Cap_BoL × (1 − fade_rate_j)^t
 fade_rate_j = f(T_ambient,j, cycles/day, DoD). Base: ~2%/year for LFP at 25°C, 1 cycle/day, 80% DoD.

Climate-adjusted degradation: fade_rate_j incorporates the Arrhenius temperature coefficient:
 fade_rate_j = fade_base × exp[E_a/R × (1/T_ref − 1/T_j(t))]
 E_a ≈ 20–30 kJ/mol (activation energy for LFP calendar degradation). T_j(t) = ambient temperature trajectory at substation j from CMIP6 (SSI I3/T2). A substation in Lecce (summer peak ~38°C rising to ~40°C by 2050) degrades ~15–25% faster than one in Bolzano (summer peak ~30°C, rising to ~31°C). This is the mechanism through which T2 enters the cost side of the NPV.

REPOWER_c(t_R) = repowering cost at trigger year t_R. Trigger: capacity fade reaches 70% of BoL (typically Year 12–13 for first repower, Year 24–25 for second). Cost: battery modules only (PCS, grid connection, civil works retained). Stochastic: future battery cost drawn from learning-curve projection. Chemistry upgrade option (solid-state, Na-ion) modelled as discrete scenarios within the repower option (Option C).

DECOM_c = decommissioning cost net of recycling credit. €15–25/kWh. EU Battery Regulation 2023/1542 mandates recycling; Li/Fe/P recovery credit reduces net cost. At 30-year horizon, heavily discounted — typically < 2% of total TCO NPV.

0.4.4   Revenue Stack & Dispatch Optimisation

The BESS generates revenue from up to 11 streams (R1–R11), subject to a physical dispatch constraint: one full charge-discharge cycle per day. The dispatch optimiser allocates this single cycle across competing revenue streams to maximise total annual revenue.

Dispatch Optimisation — Daily Problem max Σ_{i=1}^{11} R_i(t,d)   s.t.   Σ_{i∈energy} E_i(d) ≤ E_nameplate,c × η(t)   AND   contractual availability ≥ A_min,i ∀i ∈ contracted
Daily optimisation: On each day d of year t, the BESS operator allocates the available energy (E_nameplate × η(t) MWh) across revenue streams. The optimisation is decomposed into:

Layer 1 — Contracted obligations: Capacity market (R1), frequency response (R2), and CRS (R11) are availability products — the BESS must reserve capacity for contracted volumes. These reduce the energy available for other streams but provide guaranteed revenue regardless of daily conditions.
 R1: capacity market availability payment ≈ €30–75/kW/year (Italy CM 2024 auction range). Requires availability during system stress events (typically ~50–100 hours/year). BESS reserves capacity but rarely dispatched.
 R2: FCR/FFR contracted volume. BESS provides continuous frequency response while managing SoC within dead-band. Energy impact: ~5–15% of daily throughput capacity reserved for frequency regulation.
 R11: CRS availability reservation. Zero energy impact in normal operation (islanding needed only during rare extreme events).

Layer 2 — Market optimisation: Remaining energy allocated between replacement reserve (R3), wholesale arbitrage (R4), DSO flexibility (R5), and other streams via price-signal optimisation:
 R3: aFRR/mFRR activation payments. Revenue = activated volume × activation price. Activated volume depends on TSO dispatch signals — stochastic.
 R4: day-ahead + intraday arbitrage. Revenue = (P_peak − P_trough) × E_dispatched × η. The price spread is stochastic, correlated with weather (solar/wind output affects wholesale prices) and system demand.
 R5: DSO flexibility procurement. Revenue = contracted flexibility volume × flexibility price. Available in UK, emerging in IT/DE/NL.
 R7: renewable self-consumption optimisation. Revenue = avoided retail electricity cost for co-located DER consumption shifted by BESS.

Layer 3 — Emerging streams (Option F): R8 (local flexibility), R9 (LMP premium), R10 (PQaaS) modelled with probability-weighted activation in future years. Zero in base case; contribute via real options premium.

Dispatch stack hierarchy: In each daily optimisation, revenue streams are ranked by marginal revenue per MWh of BESS throughput, and the single available cycle is allocated to the highest-value combination. The stack ranking changes daily based on market conditions, creating a stochastic switching dispatch pattern that maximises expected annual revenue. The revenue model captures this via 365-day × 24-hour dispatch simulation within each Monte Carlo path.
Annual Revenue — Aggregated Rev_j,c(t) = Σ_{i=1}^{8} R_i,j,c(t) + R_11,j,c(t)
R1–R8 are energy-market revenue streams, competing for the dispatch cycle. Their sum is bounded by the energy constraint (E_nameplate × η(t) × 365).

R11 (CRS) is additive without dispatch conflict — availability product, not energy product. CRS revenue earned alongside R1–R8.

R9–R10 (LMP, PQaaS) are modelled as zero in the base-case cashflow; their value enters through Option F in the real options framework. This ensures the base NPV is conservative — no speculative revenue — while the option-adjusted V_RO captures the asymmetric upside.

Revenue evolution over 30 years: The composition of Rev(t) shifts over time. Key dynamics:
 • Capacity market revenue (R1): stable or growing (capacity scarcity increases with coal closures)
 • Frequency response (R2): declining per-MW price as BESS fleet grows (saturation effect), but volume may increase
 • Arbitrage (R4): stochastic — price spreads widen with renewable penetration (duck curve effect) but narrow with storage penetration (cannibalism effect). Net trajectory uncertain — primary source of revenue risk
 • DSO flexibility (R5): growing as DER penetration increases and DSO procurement matures
 • CRS (R11): growing with climate escalation — counter-cyclical to weather-driven degradation cost

The revenue model captures these dynamics via correlated stochastic processes for each R_i, embedded in the 10,000-path Monte Carlo. Revenue-revenue correlations (e.g., high arbitrage spreads often coincide with high ancillary service activation) are modelled through the Gaussian Copula correlation structure inherited from Layer 1.

0.4.5   Real Options Pricing Formulae

Six embedded options are priced for each substation-BESS pair. Each option adds non-negative value to the base NPV. The formulae below provide the mathematical pricing specification for implementation.

Option A — Defer (American Call) V_A,j,c = max_{τ∈[0,T]} E[e^{-rτ} × max(NPV_j,c(τ) − I_c(τ), 0)]
American call option: the right to invest in the BESS at any time τ ∈ [0, T] where T = planning horizon (~5–10 years). The investor waits if NPV(τ) < I(τ) (cost exceeds value) and invests when NPV(τ) − I(τ) is maximised.

NPV_j,c(τ) = expected NPV of the BESS if commissioned at time τ. Increases over time as revenue streams mature (DER stress grows along T1 trajectory, capacity market need increases); but decreases from τ due to forgone early-year revenue and reduced remaining asset life (30 − τ years).

I_c(τ) = CAPEX at time τ. Decreasing: BNEF learning curve reduces battery cost by ~8–10%/year through 2030, tapering to 3–5% post-2030. Stochastic: drawn from learning-curve distribution in each Monte Carlo path.

Pricing method: Binomial lattice with two stochastic state variables: (1) BESS cost trajectory (geometric Brownian motion calibrated to BNEF data, σ ≈ 12–18%/year), (2) revenue trajectory (mean-reverting with jumps, calibrated to electricity market data). Lattice steps: monthly. Backward induction identifies optimal exercise boundary.

W5/R11 deferral penalty: Climate-escalating extreme-event frequency adds a deferral cost that increases over time. Each year of delay = one year of unprotected communications-cascade risk valued at P_extended,j(t) × [C_emergency + C_economic + C_trust]. At high-I substations, this penalty can shift the optimal exercise boundary earlier by 1–3 years relative to a pure cost-trajectory model.

Typical V_A: 3–12% of NPV_base. Highest at substations where both cost decline is steep (recent battery generation, pre-cost-plateau) and revenue is rising (T1 trajectory approaching critical threshold).
Option B — Expand Duration (European Call) V_B,j,c = e^{-r×t_R1} × E[max(ΔNPV_duration,j,c − ΔI_modules, 0)]
European call exercisable at first repowering (t_R1 ≈ Year 12–13). At repowering, the investor can add duration modules (4hr → 6hr or 8hr) at incremental cost ΔI_modules.

ΔNPV_duration = incremental NPV from additional duration over remaining asset life (30 − t_R1 years). Additional duration increases: (a) arbitrage revenue (capture wider daily price spread), (b) capacity market de-rating factor (longer-duration BESS receives higher CM payment), (c) CRS contract value (longer communications bridge time → higher retainer from telcos/CP agencies).

ΔI_modules = cost of additional battery modules at t_R1. Stochastic: future battery cost projection.

Pricing: Black-Scholes analogue. Underlying = ΔNPV_duration (lognormally distributed). Strike = ΔI_modules (stochastic). Volatility calibrated from spread of capacity-market duration premium projections and CRS contract value forecasts.

Typical V_B: 1–5% of NPV_base. Highest where long-duration services are emerging (UK capacity market T-4, Italian CM with duration-differentiated clearing).
Option C — Repower (Compound Option via LSMC) V_C,j,c = LSMC(10,000 paths; Σ_copula; state = [Cap(t), Rev(t), Cost(t), T_climate(t)])
Compound option: Two sequential exercise points — first repower (~Year 12), second repower (~Year 24). The decision at t_R1 is informed by uncertainty resolution since commissioning, and creates a new option for t_R2. The chemistry chosen at t_R1 constrains (or expands) the technology set available at t_R2.

State variables at exercise point:
 • Cap(t): observed capacity fade trajectory → determines if repower trigger (70% BoL) is reached
 • Rev(t): observed revenue path → determines if repowering NPV is positive
 • Cost(t): projected battery cost at exercise → determines exercise price
 • T_climate(t): observed climate trajectory vs CMIP6 projection → Bayesian update on hazard path

LSMC procedure (Longstaff-Schwartz):
(1) Simulate 10,000 forward paths of the four state variables using the Gaussian Copula from Layer 1 (§0.3.4), ensuring correlations between cost, revenue, degradation, and climate shocks are preserved.
(2) At each exercise point, estimate the continuation value by regressing the discounted future cashflows on polynomial basis functions of the state variables (Laguerre polynomials of order 3–5).
(3) The optimal exercise decision at each point on each path: repower if immediate repower value > estimated continuation value.
(4) Average the resulting cashflows across all paths to obtain V_C.

Repower decision matrix at t_R1:
 (a) Replace with same chemistry (LFP): lowest cost, known performance, no technology risk
 (b) Upgrade chemistry (solid-state, Na-ion): higher cost, potentially better performance (higher energy density → more duration in same footprint, or lower degradation rate)
 (c) Resize (add or remove capacity): adjust to observed revenue mix and DER trajectory
 (d) Do not repower (abandon at this point → links to Option D)

W5/R11 interaction: By Year 12, observed extreme-event frequency provides a Bayesian update on the CMIP6 climate path. If events have tracked the upper tail, repowering may shift toward increased duration (for longer comms bridge time) and/or upgraded islanding switchgear. CRS revenue at Year 12 may substantially exceed commissioning estimates, justifying repowering investment uneconomic on R1–R10 alone.

Typical V_C: 8–20% of NPV_base. The single largest option component — captures the enormous uncertainty in 12–24 year technology trajectories, climate paths, and market evolution.
Option D — Abandon (American Put) V_D,j,c = max_{τ∈[1,30]} E[e^{-rτ} × max(Salvage_j,c(τ) − PV_remaining,j,c(τ), 0)]
American put option: the right to cease operations and recover salvage value at any time τ if the continuing-operations NPV falls below salvage.

Salvage_j,c(τ) = salvage value at time τ. Comprises: (a) land/site value (appreciates with general inflation), (b) grid connection residual value (depreciates but retains strategic value — a grid connection at a constrained node is increasingly scarce), (c) battery recycling credit (EU Battery Regulation mandates recovery of Li, Co, Ni, Cu, Fe — credit per kWh declines with capacity fade but remains positive), (d) PCS/inverter residual (5–10 year useful life beyond battery modules).

PV_remaining,j,c(τ) = present value at τ of all future cashflows from continuing operations (30 − τ remaining years). If PV_remaining < Salvage, the investor exercises the abandon option.

Pricing: Binomial lattice. Underlying = PV_remaining (stochastic, driven by revenue uncertainty). Strike = Salvage (partially stochastic — land value and recycling credit both uncertain). Monthly steps. Backward induction.

W5/R11 floor: Communications-resilience value creates a floor beneath PV_remaining that is independent of electricity market design. Even if R1–R10 collapse simultaneously (regime shift), W5 community value and R11 CRS revenue persist — indeed increase under climate escalation. This floor raises the threshold at which abandonment is triggered: at high-I substations, the W5/R11 floor can prevent abandonment entirely under scenarios where a pure-market BESS would be abandoned. Socially critical result — these are the communities most vulnerable to cascading failure.

Typical V_D: 2–8% of NPV_base. Highest where revenue risk is greatest (emerging markets, volatile regulatory environments) and salvage value is highest (urban sites with strategic grid connections).
Option E — Stage: Config A → Config B (American Call) V_E,j = max_{τ∈[1,T_E]} E[e^{-rτ} × max(NPV_B,j(τ) − NPV_A,j(τ) − ΔI_{A→B}(τ), 0)]
American call on the incremental value of expanding from Config A to Config B. The investor deploys Config A first, observes actual revenue performance and grid conditions, then decides whether to scale up.

NPV_B,j(τ) − NPV_A,j(τ) = incremental value of the additional 5 MW capacity at exercise time τ. Informed by τ years of operational data — the investor has resolved uncertainty about revenue performance, grid conditions, regulatory environment, and climate trajectory at this specific substation.

ΔI_{A→B}(τ) = incremental CAPEX for expanding from 4.9 MW to 9.9 MW. Includes: additional battery modules, PCS upgrade, grid connection upgrade (if required — depends on available headroom at the substation). The cost is stochastic (battery learning curve) and partially sunk (some infrastructure costs were already incurred in Config A).

Information value: Option E's primary contribution is the value of observing before committing. By deploying Config A first, the investor learns: (a) actual revenue performance at this specific substation (resolves ~60–70% of revenue uncertainty in the first 2–3 years), (b) actual degradation rate (resolves temperature-dependent fade uncertainty), (c) actual DER trajectory (observes whether T1 projection materialises), (d) actual extreme-event experience (one or two observed events provide substantial Bayesian update on W5/R11 value).

W5/R11 interaction: If an extreme event occurs during Config A operation — demonstrating communications-cascade risk in practice — the exercise trigger for Config B accelerates. Observed events validate the W5 model and strengthen the case for the larger BESS. Each additional MW extends the potential communications bridge time, increasing CRS contract value. This creates a positive feedback between climate observation and investment decision — a feature unique to the staging option.

Pricing: Binomial lattice. Underlying = incremental NPV (stochastic). Strike = incremental CAPEX (stochastic, declining). Exercise boundary identified by backward induction. T_E ≈ 5–8 years (beyond this, technology obsolescence reduces the value of expanding the original platform).

Typical V_E: 5–15% of Config A NPV_base. Highest at substations where Config A is clearly viable but Config B viability is uncertain — the staging flexibility has maximum value precisely where uncertainty is greatest.
Option F — Market Evolution: LMP + PQaaS + CRS (Compound American via LSMC) V_F,j,c = LSMC(10,000 paths; state = [𝟙_LMP(t), 𝟙_PQ(t), 𝟙_CRS(t), Rev_9(t), Rev_10(t), Rev_11(t)])
Scenario-weighted LSMC. Three independent emergence variables produce eight scenarios (2³): {∅, LMP, PQ, CRS, LMP+PQ, LMP+CRS, PQ+CRS, LMP+PQ+CRS}. Each emerges stochastically along each Monte Carlo path.

Emergence process per stream:
 𝟙_LMP(t): Poisson process with intensity λ_LMP ≈ 0.03–0.08/year (3–8% annual probability of introduction). Once emerged, persists permanently (absorbing state). λ_LMP is country-specific: UK highest (REMA review), continental EU lower.
 𝟙_PQ(t): Poisson with λ_PQ ≈ 0.04–0.10/year. Driven by DER penetration → PQ degradation → regulatory response.
 𝟙_CRS(t): Poisson with climate-path-dependent intensity: λ_CRS(t) = λ_CRS,base + κ × N_extreme,j(0:t) where N_extreme is the count of observed extreme grid failures since commissioning. κ captures the political-economy feedback: each severe failure accelerates regulatory action on resilience procurement. This correlation between trigger probability and payoff magnitude amplifies option value beyond independent-probability models.

Payoff upon emergence:
 Rev_9(t): LMP premium = locational spread × E_nameplate × η(t) × 365. Spread estimated from S1/S2/I4/I6 (congestion profile). +30–80% on R4 at congested nodes.
 Rev_10(t): PQaaS = reactive power + harmonic filtering + sag compensation revenue. From V1/V2 (PQ degradation level). €20–150k/year per substation.
 Rev_11(t): CRS = telco backup + CP contracts + NIS2 resilience-as-a-service. From I/T/C (climate hazard × outage tail × comms density). €17–400k/year per substation. Requires islanding switchgear (ΔI_island) if not already installed — creating a nested option within Option F.

LSMC pricing: Same engine as Option C. 10,000 paths. Copula-correlated state evolution. At each annual decision node, the LSMC engine evaluates: should the BESS invest in islanding switchgear now (to capture CRS upon emergence), or wait? The nested option structure means Option F is a compound option — the decision to invest in islanding capability is itself an option whose exercise depends on the CRS emergence probability.

Typical V_F: 10–35% of NPV_base at SSI-identified hotspots. Decomposition: LMP component 5–15% (at high-S/T nodes), PQaaS component 3–10% (at high-V nodes), CRS component 5–15% (at high-I/T/C nodes). The three dimensions are largely orthogonal — a substation can score high on all three, yielding the maximum premium. Substations with no congestion, good PQ, and low climate hazard contribute ~0% from Option F — but these substations also have low base NPV, so Option F preferentially amplifies value at the most attractive sites.

0.4.6   Integrated Valuation & Monte Carlo Propagation

The final valuation output integrates all Layer 2 components into a single option-adjusted NPV per substation per configuration, propagated through the full 10,000-iteration Monte Carlo engine.

Base NPV (Traditional DCF) NPV_base,j,c = −CAPEX_c + Σ_{t=1}^{30} [Rev_j,c(t) − OPEX_c(t)] / (1+WACC)^t − REPOWER/DECOM (discounted)
This is the traditional DCF valuation — the "floor" that any competent analyst would compute. It captures all R1–R8 + R11 revenue streams, all TCO costs including climate-adjusted degradation and repowering, discounted at project-specific WACC. It does NOT capture the value of managerial flexibility (options) or speculative future revenue streams (R9/R10). NPV_base is deliberately conservative.
Option-Adjusted NPV V_RO,j,c = NPV_base,j,c + V_A + V_B + V_C + V_D + V_E + V_F
Option values always ≥ 0. They represent pure upside — the value of managerial flexibility that DCF ignores.

Typical option premium: V_RO / NPV_base − 1 ≈ 20–50%. Decomposition:
 V_A (Defer): 3–12%
 V_B (Expand): 1–5%
 V_C (Repower): 8–20% — largest single option
 V_D (Abandon): 2–8%
 V_E (Stage): 5–15% (on Config A NPV; zero for Config B since Config B is already full-scale)
 V_F (Market Evolution): 10–35% at hotspots; near-zero at unconstrained/low-risk sites

The premium is not a modelling artefact — it captures genuine economic value from flexibility in a system where price-volume interactions are nonlinear, regime shifts can permanently alter the revenue landscape, path dependence is total, and resilience value grows with climate escalation.
Combined Valuation Output (per substation, per configuration) V_total,j,c = {V_DNO,j,c, V_Community,j,c, NPV_base,j,c, V_RO,j,c, IRR_j,c, Payback_j,c}
The output vector for each substation-config pair comprises:

V_DNO,j,c: 30-year NPV of DNO value stack (D1–D4) at regulated WACC. Audience: DNO procurement teams, DSO flexibility markets, regulatory submissions.

V_Community,j,c: 30-year NPV of community value stack (W1–W5) at social discount rate (3.0%). Audience: policymakers, EU funding applications, regulatory engagement. Reported with physical metrics (ΔSAIDI, tCO₂, FTE, blackout-hours avoided, population protected).

NPV_base,j,c: Traditional DCF from investor perspective. Revenue (R1–R8 + R11) minus TCO. Audience: infrastructure investors, project finance, lease underwriting.

V_RO,j,c: Option-adjusted NPV = NPV_base + options A–F. Audience: sophisticated investors, infrastructure funds, lease-rate optimisation.

IRR_j,c: Pre-tax unlevered IRR at P50 Monte Carlo path. Also reported: levered IRR at 70:30 D:E structure. P10 and P90 IRR for risk assessment.

Payback_j,c: Time to NPV = 0 at P50. Also reported: P10 and P90 payback periods.

All outputs reported with Monte Carlo uncertainty bands: {P10, P50, P90, mean, σ} from 10,000 iterations. The copula structure (§0.3.4) ensures that inter-metric correlations and tail dependencies are propagated through every calculation — a substation that scores poorly on multiple SSI dimensions simultaneously will show wider uncertainty bands and higher tail risk, which is the correct financial characterisation.
Monte Carlo Propagation Pipeline For k = 1, ..., 10,000:   SSI^(k) → {D^(k), W^(k)} → {TCO^(k), Rev^(k)} → {NPV^(k), Options^(k)} → V_total^(k)
End-to-end pipeline for each Monte Carlo iteration k:

Step 1 — Layer 1 draw: Draw SSI sub-metric vector x^(k) from the Gaussian Copula (§0.3.4). This produces a correlated draw of all 20 sub-metrics respecting marginal distributions and inter-metric dependencies (including tail dependence from the Clayton mixture).

Step 2 — Value-add computation: Pass x^(k) through the DNO formulae (D1–D5) and Community formulae (W1–W5) from §0.4.1–0.4.2. Each D and W component receives stochastic SSI inputs — the formulas are deterministic given inputs, but the inputs are stochastic draws from the copula.

Step 3 — TCO computation: Draw stochastic cost parameters (CAPEX learning curve, degradation rate including Arrhenius temperature adjustment from I3^(k)/T2^(k), repowering cost trajectory). Compute TCO^(k) per §0.4.3.

Step 4 — Revenue simulation: Simulate 30-year revenue path for each stream R_i. Draw wholesale prices, ancillary prices, capacity market prices from correlated stochastic processes. Run dispatch optimiser per §0.4.4 to allocate the daily cycle across streams. Compute Rev^(k)(t) for t = 1, ..., 30.

Step 5 — Base NPV: NPV_base^(k) = −CAPEX^(k) + Σ [Rev^(k)(t) − OPEX^(k)(t)] / (1+r)^t.

Step 6 — Option pricing: Within each Monte Carlo path k, evaluate Options A–F using the sub-path's realised state variables. For lattice-based options (A, B, D, E): the underlying's volatility is calibrated from the cross-path dispersion at each node. For LSMC options (C, F): the 10,000 primary paths serve as the LSMC sample — no nested simulation required.

Step 7 — Aggregate: V_total^(k) = {V_DNO^(k), V_Community^(k), NPV_base^(k), V_RO^(k), IRR^(k), Payback^(k)}.

Step 8 — Statistics: After all 10,000 iterations: compute P10, P50, P90, mean, σ for each output. Report confidence intervals. Identify which SSI sub-metrics contribute most to output variance (Sobol indices from the Monte Carlo sample).

Computational performance: Steps 1–7 execute in ~0.5–1.0 seconds per substation per config on modern GPU. Full Italian scan (39,234 substations × 2 configs × 10,000 iterations) ≈ 11–22 hours. The bottleneck is Step 4 (365-day dispatch simulation within each path). Incremental country scans benefit from pre-computed copula Σ and transfer-learned dispatch models.
Layer 2 completeness verification: The valuation architecture provides a closed-form or simulation-based formula for every output number produced by the SSI-ENN. Every formula traces back through Layer 2 to Layer 1 inputs (SSI sub-metrics + market data + regulatory parameters). No output is a "black box" — any V_total component can be decomposed into its constituent formulae, parameters, and source data via the Layer 1 data flow matrix (§0.3.6) and the Layer 2 formulae documented above. This full traceability is an investment-grade requirement: any auditor, regulator, or investor can follow the chain from a published NPV number back to the raw SAIDI data, CMIP6 projection, or wholesale price input that drove it.

0.4.7   Layer 2 Phase 1 — Implementation Status

Layer 2 Phase 1 has been implemented in the ssi_layer2 Python package (v0.1.0), covering the three deterministic valuation engines: DNO value stack (D1–D5), Community value stack (W1–W5), and Total Cost of Ownership (TCO). The package also includes Italian electricity market data (GME wholesale prices, ARERA tariffs, ancillary service rates, EU ETS carbon prices) and BESS technology cost curves (LFP/NMC learning curves 2020–2050). Phase 2 (Revenue Stack R1–R11, LSMC, Options A–F) is the next implementation milestone.

Repository: IkengaTest/ssi-layer2 (GitHub, private). 21 source files, 6,132 lines of Python. 128 unit + integration tests, 100% passing. Dual-config architecture operational: every substation evaluated for Config A (4.9 MW / 19.6 MWh) and Config B (9.9 MW / 39.6 MWh).

Key Implementation Decisions

NPV discounting architecture: All D1–D5 values are discounted at the regulated WACC (Italy: 5.5%, configurable per country). All W1–W5 values use the social discount rate (3.0%). This dual-rate approach reflects the different value recipients: DNO values accrue to the regulated utility and should reflect the regulated cost of capital; community values accrue to society and should reflect the social time preference rate. The TCO model uses the project WACC for investor-perspective NPV.

BESS current reduction formula: The D2 loss reduction engine uses the MV-grid current reduction formula: ΔI = P_BESS × 10⁶ / (√3 × 20kV × 0.9 PF). This is then applied to the I²R loss formula: Loss_reduction = LF × (2ΔI/I − (ΔI/I)²) × E_annual × C_loss, where LF is the territory-specific loss factor from ARERA data.

Arrhenius temperature-dependent degradation: The TCO engine implements Arrhenius-adjusted capacity fade: each °C above 25°C reference temperature accelerates calendar degradation by 6.7% (Ea/R ≈ 6,000 K for LFP). This produces substation-specific degradation profiles when combined with CMIP6 SSP2-4.5 temperature projections from the Layer 1 T-component. Augmentation is triggered when SoH drops below 80%, with replacement costs interpolated from the BNEF learning curve.

Section 0.5

Layer 3 — Neural Network Architecture ✓ Implemented v0.4.0–v0.5.0

Layer 3 is the scalability engine. Layers 1 and 2 produce precise, bottom-up valuations for 39,234 Italian substations where the full SSI v4.0 dataset exists. Replicating this analytical depth for ~500,000+ substations across 28 countries would require complete SSI-equivalent data for each — which will take years to assemble. Layer 3 bridges this gap: it learns the mapping f: X → Y from available input features X to full valuation outputs Y by training on the Italian dataset where both are complete, then transfers this learned mapping to countries where only partial features are available.

This section provides the formal mathematical specification of the neural network — from input vector construction through architecture, loss functions, transfer-learning protocol, uncertainty quantification, and explainability. The design is dictated by three constraints: (a) tabular data with heterogeneous feature types (continuous, categorical, binary, ordinal), (b) the need for calibrated uncertainty bounds rather than point estimates, and (c) graceful degradation when features are missing (the defining challenge of cross-country transfer).

0.5.1   Input Feature Vector

The NN input is a feature vector x_j ∈ ℝ^d constructed for each substation j. The dimensionality d varies by data tier: d = d_full (Italian substations with complete SSI data) or d_partial < d_full (non-Italian substations with sparse data, where missing features are handled by the missingness protocol in §0.5.3).

Feature Vector Construction x_j = [x_SSI,j ⊕ x_geo,j ⊕ x_climate,j ⊕ x_grid,j ⊕ x_market,j ⊕ x_reg,j ⊕ x_econ,j ⊕ x_DER,j ⊕ x_digital,j]
⊕ denotes concatenation. Each sub-vector:

x_SSI,j ∈ ℝ^{26}: SSI v4.0.2 component scores (6: C, V, I, E, S, T) + individual sub-metric scores (20: C1–C4, V, I1–I9, E1–E2, S1–S3, T1). All normalised to [0,1] via the empirical CDF transform (§0.3.3). This block carries the highest information density — it is the distilled output of the entire SSI Index.

x_geo,j ∈ ℝ^{8}: Latitude, longitude (both standardised to [−1,1] over the country's bounding box), elevation (m, log-transformed), terrain roughness index (from SRTM 30m DEM), urban/rural classification (one-hot: 3 classes — urban/peri-urban/rural), distance to coast (km, log-transformed), distance to nearest 132kV+ substation (km — proxy for transmission-system proximity).

x_climate,j ∈ ℝ^{12}: CMIP6 SSP2-4.5 projections at substation location: mean annual temperature (°C), summer peak temperature (°C), annual precipitation (mm), extreme precipitation frequency (events/yr > 95th percentile), wind speed 10m annual mean (m/s), annual degree-days cooling (CDD), annual degree-days heating (HDD). Each reported at 3 time horizons: baseline (2010–2030), mid-century (2030–2050), late-century (2050–2070) — but compressed to baseline + Δ_mid + Δ_late to reduce collinearity. Plus: composite IRI hazard scores I1, I2, I3 (already in x_SSI, but here augmented with the raw meteorological drivers).

x_grid,j ∈ ℝ^{9}: Substation count per 50km radius (= I4 scaled), average MV feeder length (km), MV voltage level (20kV/10kV/other — one-hot), transformer rated capacity (kVA, log-transformed), transformer age (years — = I5), meshing ratio (= I6), hosting capacity utilisation (= S1), number of feeders, estimated annual throughput (MWh, from load profile).

x_market,j ∈ ℝ^{10}: Country/zone-level market features: capacity market availability (binary) + clearing price (€/kW/yr), FCR price 12-month rolling mean (€/MW/h), aFRR price mean, wholesale price mean + volatility (σ_DA, annualised), wholesale price spread (P90−P10 daily), DSO flexibility procurement availability (ordinal: none/pilot/active), LMP likelihood score (0–1, from regulatory trajectory assessment).

x_reg,j ∈ ℝ^{7}: Country-level regulatory features: grid connection cost regime (ordinal: shallow/shallowish/deep), planning permission timeline (months), quality regulation penalty rate (€/MWh unserved or €/violation), renewable support scheme type (one-hot: FiT/FiP/CfD/auction/none), BESS-specific regulatory incentive (binary), NIS2 transposition maturity (ordinal: 0–3), revenue stacking permitted (binary).

x_econ,j ∈ ℝ^{6}: NUTS-3 economic features: GDP per capita (PPS, log), GVA per worker (log), unemployment rate (%), electricity price industrial (€/MWh), electricity price domestic (€/MWh), population density (inhabitants/km², log).

x_DER,j ∈ ℝ^{5}: Regional DER features: installed solar capacity per capita (kW/person), installed wind capacity per capita, EV registrations per 1000 inhabitants, heat pump installations per 1000 households, DER growth rate last 3 years (CAGR %).

x_digital,j ∈ ℝ^{6}: Digital infrastructure: DESI composite score, FTTP coverage (% premises), 5G coverage (% area), distance to nearest data centre (km, log), base station density (towers/km² within substation service area), broadband speed median (Mbps, log).

Total dimensionality: d_full = 26 + 8 + 12 + 9 + 10 + 7 + 6 + 5 + 6 = 89 features. For Italian substations (full SSI data), all 89 are available. For Tier 1 non-Italian countries: typically 55–70 features available. For Tier 3 countries: 40–55 features. The transfer-learning protocol (§0.5.3) handles this gracefully.
Feature Preprocessing Pipeline x̂_j = Φ_preprocess(x_j) = [normalise → encode_categorical → impute_missing → mask_generate]
Step 1 — Normalisation: Continuous features are rank-normalised to U[0,1] via empirical CDF (same transform as §0.3.3 — ensures consistency between Layer 1 SSI scoring and Layer 3 NN input). This is preferred over z-scoring because many features are heavily skewed (SAIDI is right-tailed, DER capacity is zero-inflated). Rank normalisation guarantees uniform marginals regardless of distribution shape.

Step 2 — Categorical encoding: Ordinal features (e.g., territory class, regulation regime) are encoded as integers preserving order. Nominal categories (e.g., voltage level, support scheme type) are one-hot encoded. Binary features passed through unchanged.

Step 3 — Imputation: For the GBT component: no imputation needed — XGBoost/LightGBM natively handle missing values by learning optimal split directions for absent features. For the DNN component: missing features are imputed with the training-set median and flagged via a binary missingness indicator (see §0.5.3). The median is a robust default; the missingness indicator allows the network to learn that the imputed value carries lower information content.

Step 4 — Missingness mask: Generate binary mask vector m_j ∈ {0,1}^d where m_j,f = 1 if feature f is observed and 0 if missing. This mask is concatenated with the input for the DNN: the effective DNN input is [x̂_j ⊕ m_j] ∈ ℝ^{2d}. The mask enables the DNN to learn feature-availability-conditional predictions — critical for transfer learning where non-Italian substations have systematically different missingness patterns.

0.5.2   Ensemble Architecture: GBT + DNN

The architecture is a weighted ensemble of two complementary model families. Gradient-Boosted Trees (GBT) excel at tabular data with heterogeneous features, automatic feature interaction discovery, and native missing-value handling. Deep Neural Networks (DNN) excel at learning smooth nonlinear mappings, transfer learning across domains, and uncertainty quantification via dropout. The ensemble averages both for robustness — exploiting the fact that GBT and DNN tend to make different errors.

GBT Component — XGBoost / LightGBM ŷ_GBT,j = Σ_{m=1}^{M} η · f_m(x_j)   where f_m ∈ F (regression trees)
Objective function (per output head h):
L_GBT^(h) = Σ_j ℓ(y_j^(h), ŷ_j^(h)) + Σ_m Ω(f_m)

ℓ: loss function. For regression heads (V_DNO, V_Community, V_RO): Huber loss with δ = 1.5 × σ_target. Huber combines MSE for small errors with MAE for large errors — robust to outlier substations (extreme-valued sites that are disproportionately important for investment decisions but would dominate MSE training).
ℓ_Huber(y, ŷ) = ½(y−ŷ)² if |y−ŷ| ≤ δ, else δ|y−ŷ| − ½δ²

For the config classifier head: cross-entropy loss.
ℓ_CE(y, ŷ) = −Σ_k y_k log(ŷ_k) for k ∈ {A, B, Neither}

Regularisation Ω:
Ω(f) = γ · T_leaves + ½λ · Σ w²_leaf + α · Σ |w_leaf|
γ = 0.1–1.0 (minimum loss reduction per split), T_leaves = number of leaves (controls tree complexity),
λ = 1.0–10.0 (L2 regularisation on leaf weights), α = 0.0–0.5 (L1 sparsity on leaf weights).

Key hyperparameters:
M = 500–2000 boosting rounds (early stopping on validation loss with patience = 50).
η = 0.01–0.05 (learning rate — low for stability; compensated by more rounds).
max_depth = 6–10 (tree depth — controls interaction order: depth d captures d-way interactions).
subsample = 0.7–0.9 (row subsampling per round — stochastic gradient boosting for regularisation).
colsample_bytree = 0.6–0.8 (column subsampling per tree — prevents over-reliance on dominant features).
min_child_weight = 10–50 (minimum Hessian sum in a leaf — prevents splits on tiny subgroups).

Missing value handling: XGBoost/LightGBM learn a default split direction (left or right) for each feature at each tree node when the feature is absent. This is learned during training — the algorithm evaluates both directions and chooses the one that minimises the loss. No imputation needed. This property is essential for cross-country deployment where feature availability varies systematically.

Separate GBT per output head: 7 independent GBT models, one per target (V_DNO(A), V_DNO(B), V_Community(A), V_Community(B), V_RO(A), V_RO(B), config classifier). Independent training allows each head to learn its own feature-importance structure — D1 deferral value depends primarily on I/C/T features, while W2 carbon value depends primarily on S/T features. Shared training would force a single feature-importance ranking across all heads.
DNN Component — 4-Layer MLP with Residual Connections h_0 = [x̂_j ⊕ m_j] ∈ ℝ^{2d} → h_1 → h_2 → h_3 → h_4 → {ŷ_h}_h=1..7
Architecture:
h_1 = ReLU(BN(W_1 · h_0 + b_1))   dim: 2d → 512
h_2 = ReLU(BN(W_2 · h_1 + b_2)) + W_skip,1 · h_0   dim: 512 → 256 (with skip connection from input)
h_3 = ReLU(BN(W_3 · h_2 + b_3))   dim: 256 → 128
h_4 = ReLU(BN(W_4 · h_3 + b_3)) + W_skip,2 · h_2   dim: 128 → 64 (with skip connection from h_2)

BN = Batch Normalisation (applied before activation). Stabilises training with heterogeneous input scales.
ReLU = Rectified Linear Unit: max(0, x). Standard for tabular data — alternatives (GELU, SiLU) tested but no consistent improvement on this data type.
Skip connections (residual): h_2 receives a linear projection of h_0, h_4 receives a projection of h_2. Prevents gradient degradation in the deeper layers and provides the network with "raw" features alongside learned representations at each stage.

Layer dimensions rationale: The 512 → 256 → 128 → 64 funnel compresses the 2d-dimensional input (2 × 86 = 172 for full Italian data) through progressively narrower representations. The bottleneck at h_4 (64-dimensional) forces the network to learn a compact latent representation of the substation's valuation-relevant characteristics. This bottleneck representation is the feature space in which transfer learning operates — see §0.5.3.

Dropout: Applied after each hidden layer during training with probability p_drop = 0.15–0.25. Serves two purposes: (1) regularisation during training, (2) Monte Carlo uncertainty quantification during inference (§0.5.5). Dropout is NOT applied to skip connections — these preserve information flow during both training and inference.

Total trainable parameters: ~250K–350K depending on input dimensionality. Small by deep-learning standards — appropriate for a tabular dataset of ~39K observations. Larger networks overfit; smaller networks lose the capacity to capture complex feature interactions (e.g., the three-way interaction between DER saturation, network topology, and wholesale price volatility that determines arbitrage value at congested nodes).
Ensemble Combination ŷ_ensemble,j^(h) = w_GBT · ŷ_GBT,j^(h) + w_DNN · ŷ_DNN,j^(h)   where w_GBT + w_DNN = 1
Combination weights: Learned by minimising validation-set loss on the Italian holdout regions. Not fixed a priori — the optimal weighting varies by output head and data tier.

Typical weights (Italian full data): w_GBT ≈ 0.55–0.65, w_DNN ≈ 0.35–0.45. GBT dominates when full tabular features are available — consistent with empirical findings that tree-based methods outperform DNNs on tabular data (Grinsztajn et al., NeurIPS 2022).

Weights shift with data sparsity: As features become missing (non-Italian countries), w_DNN increases because: (a) the DNN's missingness mask + imputation protocol is more principled than GBT's default-direction splits for systematically missing features (entire feature blocks absent, not random missingness), (b) the DNN's transfer-learned lower layers carry forward Italian-learned representations that compensate for absent features. At Tier 3 countries (40–55 of 86 features available), weights may shift to w_GBT ≈ 0.40, w_DNN ≈ 0.60.

Ensemble benefit: GBT and DNN make correlated but non-identical errors. The ensemble reduces variance by averaging over two different inductive biases (piecewise-constant decision boundaries for GBT vs smooth nonlinear surfaces for DNN). Empirical expectation: ensemble R² ≈ 0.02–0.05 higher than either component alone, with the improvement concentrated in the tails (extreme-value substations where model disagreement is largest — exactly the substations investors care about most).

0.5.3   Transfer Learning & Feature Missingness

Transfer learning is the mechanism that enables the SSI-ENN to value substations in countries where the full 86-feature vector is not available. The core insight: grid physics is universal (topology determines reliability, DER determines congestion, climate determines hazard risk — regardless of country), but market and regulatory parameters are country-specific. The DNN architecture exploits this by learning universal grid relationships in its lower layers and country-specific mappings in its upper layers.

Three-Stage Transfer Learning Protocol Stage 1: θ* = argmin_θ L(f_θ(X_IT), Y_IT) — full training on Italian data
Stage 1 — Base model (Italy, full data):
Train the complete ensemble (GBT + DNN) on all 39,234 Italian substations. X_IT ∈ ℝ^{39,234 × 86} (full feature matrix). Y_IT ∈ ℝ^{39,234 × 7} (7 target variables from Phases 1+2). Optimise to convergence via 5-fold cross-validation. This produces the "teacher" model with all parameters θ* = {θ_GBT*, θ_DNN*}. The Italian model is the highest-accuracy configuration — it serves as the reference against which all transfer-learned models are benchmarked.

Stage 2 — Feature ablation (Italy, simulated sparsity):
Re-train on the same Italian data but with features progressively dropped to simulate data availability in non-Italian countries. For each target data tier (Tier 1, 2, 3), define a feature-availability mask M_tier ∈ {0,1}^{86} reflecting which features are typically available in that tier. Train a separate model for each tier: θ*_tier = argmin_θ L(f_θ(X_IT ⊙ M_tier), Y_IT). This produces:
 (a) Robustness map: For each feature f, the accuracy degradation when f is removed: ΔR²_f = R²_full − R²_{−f}. Features with large ΔR² are acquisition priorities for Phase 4 country expansion.
 (b) Tier-calibrated models: Models pre-adapted to the missingness patterns typical of each country tier, which serve as the starting weights for Stage 3 fine-tuning.

Stage 3 — Transfer fine-tuning (Country X, partial data):
For each new country X, the transfer procedure is:
(1) Identify which features are available → determine effective tier and closest M_tier mask.
(2) Initialise DNN weights from the Stage 2 tier-calibrated model: θ_DNN,X ← θ*_DNN,tier.
(3) Freeze lower layers (h_1, h_2): these encode universal grid-physics relationships learned from Italian data. They do not change during country-specific fine-tuning.
(4) Fine-tune upper layers (h_3, h_4, output heads): these adapt to country-specific market structure, regulatory parameters, and the local relationship between grid characteristics and valuation.
(5) Fine-tuning data for Country X: any available local BESS project data (observed revenue for operational assets), plus the country's available SSI-equivalent features for its substation population.
(6) If no local BESS project data exists (no observed Y_X): the tier-calibrated model from Stage 2 is used as-is, with widened uncertainty bounds (§0.5.5) reflecting zero local calibration. This is the "cold start" scenario — the model still produces useful predictions, but with P10/P90 bands that are 2–3× wider than for calibrated countries.

For the GBT component: re-train from scratch on Country X data (GBTs do not transfer-learn in the classical sense, but benefit from hyperparameter transfer: the optimal regularisation settings from Italy are used as starting points for Country X tuning).
Feature Missingness Handling x̂_j = x_j ⊙ m_j + μ_train ⊙ (1 − m_j)   ;   DNN input: [x̂_j ⊕ m_j] ∈ ℝ^{2d}
Two types of missingness:

Type 1 — Random missingness (within-country): Individual substations missing individual features due to data gaps (e.g., one substation's V1 measurement unavailable). Handled by standard imputation: x̂_f = μ_train,f (training set median for feature f). Random missingness is uncommon — most SSI data is available at the territorial level for all substations within a country, or unavailable for all.

Type 2 — Systematic missingness (cross-country): Entire feature blocks absent for all substations in a country (e.g., no substation-level SAIDI in Germany — only national aggregate). This is the dominant missingness mode in cross-country transfer. Handled by:
 (a) For GBT: native missing-value handling (learned default split direction).
